921

922 <section id="_1">

923

924 <h2>

925General Information About Childhood Hodgkin Lymphoma</h2>

926

927 <div id="_section_1" class="pdq-sections">

928<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

929<h6>

930In This Section</h6>

931<ul>

932<li>

933<a href="#_423_toc">

934Overview of Childhood Hodgkin Lymphoma </a>

935</li>

936<li>

937<a href="#_420_toc">

938Epidemiology<ul>

939<li>

940<a href="#_615_toc">

941EBV and Hodgkin lymphoma</a>

942</li>

943<li>

944<a href="#_619_toc">

945Immunodeficiency and Hodgkin lymphoma</a>

946</li>

947</ul>

948</a>

949</li>

950<li>

951<a href="#_422_toc">

952Clinical Presentation</a>

953</li>

954<li>

955<a href="#_548_toc">

956Prognostic Factors<ul>

957<li>

958<a href="#_1831_toc">

959Pretreatment factors</a>

960</li>

961<li>

962<a href="#_1837_toc">

963Response to initial chemotherapy</a>

964</li>

965</ul>

966</a>

967</li>

968</ul>

969</nav>

970<div class="pdq-hp-patient-toggle">

971<a href="/types/lymphoma/patient/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq">

972Go to Patient Version</a>

973</div>

974<p id="_268" tabindex="-1">

975Dramatic improvements in survival have been achieved for children and adolescents with cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9761</a>

977] Between 2011 and 2017, the 5-year overall survival rate was 98% for patients younger than 20 years with Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

9782</a>

979] </p>

980<section id="_423">

981<h3 id="_423_toc">

982Overview of Childhood Hodgkin Lymphoma </h3>

983<p id="_162" tabindex="-1">

984Childhood Hodgkin lymphoma is one of the few pediatric malignancies that shares aspects of its biology and natural history with an adult cancer. When initial treatment approaches for children were modeled after those used for adults, substantial morbidities resulted from unacceptably high radiation doses. As a result, new strategies using chemotherapy and lower-dose radiation were developed.</p>

985<p id="_1826" tabindex="-1">

986 Approximately 90% to 95% of children with Hodgkin lymphoma can be cured, prompting increased attention to therapy that lessens long-term morbidity for these patients. Contemporary treatment programs use a risk-based and response-adapted approach in which patients receive multiagent chemotherapy, with or without low-dose involved-field or involved-site radiation therapy.

987

988

989

990

991 Prognostic factors used to determine chemotherapy intensity include cancer stage, presence or absence of B symptoms (fever, weight loss, and night sweats), bulky disease, extranodal involvement, and/or erythrocyte sedimentation rate.</p>

992</section>

993<section id="_420">

994<h3 id="_420_toc">

995Epidemiology</h3>

996<p id="_163" tabindex="-1">

997Hodgkin lymphoma accounts for 6% of childhood cancers. In the United States, the incidence of Hodgkin lymphoma is age related and is highest among adolescents aged 15 to 19 years (33.3 cases per 1 million per year). Children aged 10 to 14 years, 5 to 9 years, and 0 to 4 years have approximately threefold, tenfold, and 20-fold lower rates of Hodgkin lymphoma, respectively, than do adolescents.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9983</a>

999] In developing countries, there is a similar incidence rate in young adults but a much higher incidence rate in childhood.[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

10004</a>

1001] </p>

1002<p id="_705" tabindex="-1">

1003Hodgkin lymphoma has the following unique epidemiological features:</p>

1004<div class="pdq-content-list">

1005<ul id="_669">

1006<li>

1007<strong>

1008Bimodal age distribution. </strong>

1009Bimodal age distribution differs geographically and ethnically. In industrialized countries, the early peak occurs in the middle-to-late 20s and the second peak after age 50 years. In low-income countries, the early peak occurs before adolescence.[<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

10105</a>

1011]</li>

1012<li>

1013<strong>

1014Male-to-female ratio. </strong>

1015This ratio varies markedly by age. In children younger than 10 years, the incidence of Hodgkin lymphoma is twofold to threefold higher in males than in females. In children aged 10 to 14 years, the incidence is approximately 1.2-fold higher in males than in females. In adolescents aged 15 to 19 years, the incidence is similar for males and females.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

10162</a>

1017]</li>

1018<li>

1019<strong>

1020Age cohorts. </strong>

1021Hodgkin lymphoma can be segregated into the following three age cohorts because of the variation in etiologies and histological subtypes (see <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_1813">

1022Table 1</a>

1023):<div class="pdq-content-list">

1024<ul id="_670" class="list-dash">

1025<li>

1026<strong>

1027Children:</strong>

1028 More males than females are affected in the youngest age cohort, especially in children younger than 10 years.<p id="_2040" tabindex="-1">

1029Individuals aged 14 years and younger have a higher prevalence of nodular lymphocyte-predominant disease and Epstein-Barr virus (EBV)&#8211;associated mixed-cellularity disease. EBV-associated Hodgkin lymphoma increases in prevalence in association with larger family size and lower socioeconomic status.[<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

10305</a>

1031]</p>

1032<p id="_1827" tabindex="-1">

1033 Early exposure to common infections in early childhood appears to decrease the risk of Hodgkin lymphoma, most likely by maturation of cellular immunity.[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

10346</a>

1035,<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

10367</a>

1037]</p>

1038</li>

1039<li>

1040<strong>

1041Adolescents and young adults:</strong>

1042 Hodgkin lymphoma in individuals aged 15 to 34 years is associated with a higher socioeconomic status in industrialized countries, increased sibship size, and earlier birth order.[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

10438</a>

1044] The lower risk of Hodgkin lymphoma observed in young adults with multiple older, but not younger, siblings, is consistent with the hypothesis that early exposure to viral infection (which the siblings bring home from school, for example) may play a role in the pathogenesis of the disease.[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

10456</a>

1046]<p id="_1829" tabindex="-1">

1047 Nodular-sclerosing Hodgkin lymphoma is the most common subtype, followed by mixed cellularity.</p>

1048</li>

1049<li>

1050<strong>

1051Older adults:</strong>

1052 Hodgkin lymphoma most commonly presents in individuals aged 55 to 74 years. This group has a higher risk of lymphocyte-depleted Hodgkin lymphoma. The treatment of older adults is not discussed in this summary. For more information, see <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/adult-hodgkin-treatment-pdq">

1053Hodgkin Lymphoma Treatment</a>

1054.</li>

1055</ul>

1056</div>

1057</li>

1058<li>

1059<strong>

1060Family history. </strong>

1061A family history of Hodgkin lymphoma in siblings or parents has been associated with an increased risk of this disease.[<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

10629</a>

1063,<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

106410</a>

1065] In a population-based study that evaluated risk of familial classical Hodgkin lymphoma by relationship, histology, age, and sex, the cumulative risk of Hodgkin lymphoma was 0.6%, representing a 3.3-fold increased risk compared with the general population.[<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

106611</a>

1067] The risk in siblings was significantly higher than the risk in parents and/or offspring. The risk in sisters was higher than the risk in brothers or siblings of opposite sex. The lifetime risk of Hodgkin lymphoma was higher when first-degree relatives were diagnosed before age 30 years.</li>

1068<li>

1069<strong>

1070Genetic susceptibility.</strong>

1071 A study of twins affected by Hodgkin lymphoma showed that monozygotic twins, but not dizygotic twins, have a greatly increased risk of Hodgkin lymphoma (standardized incidence ratio of approximately 100). This finding supports the idea that genetic susceptibility underlies Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

107212</a>

1073] A meta-analysis of genome-wide association studies identified 18 risk loci for Hodgkin lymphoma, further validating the major role of genetic susceptibility. Genes putatively associated with the risk loci affected three general biological processes: germinal center reaction, T-cell differentiation and function, and constitutive nuclear factor kappa-light-chain-enhancer of activated B cells (NF-kappaB) activation.[<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

107413</a>

1075]<p id="_2073" tabindex="-1">

1076A comprehensive whole genome sequencing effort was conducted in 234 individuals with and without Hodgkin lymphoma, selected from 36 pedigrees that had two or more first-degree affected relatives.[<a href="#cit/section_1.14">

107714</a>

1078] Using linkage and a tiered variant prioritization algorithm, 44 Hodgkin lymphoma pathogenic risk variants were identified (33 coding variants and 11 noncoding variants). A recurrent coding variant was seen in <em class="gene-name">

1079KDR</em>

1080, and a 5&#8217; untranslated region variant was seen in <em class="gene-name">

1081KLHDC8B</em>

1082&#8212;both of which have previously been identified. Two new noncoding variants were seen in <em class="gene-name">

1083PAX5</em>

1084 (intron 5) and <em class="gene-name">

1085GATA3</em>

1086 (intron 3). In addition, multiple unrelated families harbored novel loss of function variants in <em class="gene-name">

1087POLR1E</em>

1088 and stop-gain variants in <em class="gene-name">

1089IRF7</em>

1090 and <em class="gene-name">

1091EEF2KMT</em>

1092. These findings validated previous studies and identified additional germline variants associated with an increased risk of Hodgkin lymphoma.</p>

1093</li>

1094</ul>

1095</div>

1291<section id="_615">

1292<h4 id="_615_toc">

1293EBV and Hodgkin lymphoma</h4>

1294<p id="_616" tabindex="-1">

1295EBV has been implicated in the etiology of some cases of Hodgkin lymphoma. Patients with Hodgkin lymphoma may have high EBV titers, suggesting that a previous infection with EBV may precede the development of Hodgkin lymphoma in some cases. EBV genetic material can be detected in Hodgkin and Reed-Sternberg (HRS) cells from some patients with Hodgkin lymphoma, most commonly in those with mixed-cellularity disease.[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

129616</a>

1297] In children and adolescents with intermediate-risk Hodgkin lymphoma, EBV DNA in cell-free blood correlated with the presence of EBV in the tumor. EBV DNA found in cell-free blood 8 days after the initiation of therapy predicted an inferior event-free survival (EFS).[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

129816</a>

1299]</p>

1300<p id="_706" tabindex="-1">

1301The incidence of EBV-associated Hodgkin lymphoma also shows the following distinct epidemiological features:</p>

1302<div class="pdq-content-list">

1303<ul id="_617">

1304<li>

1305<strong>

1306Histology.</strong>

1307 EBV positivity is most commonly observed in tumors with mixed-cellularity histology and is almost never seen in patients with lymphocyte-predominant histology.[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

130817</a>

1309,<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

131018</a>

1311]</li>

1312<li>

1313<strong>

1314Age.</strong>

1315 EBV positivity is more common in children younger than 10 years than in adolescents and young adults.[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

131617</a>

1317,<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

131818</a>

1319] </li>

1320<li>

1321<strong>

1322Developing countries.</strong>

1323 The incidence of EBV tumor cell positivity for Hodgkin lymphoma in developed countries ranges from 15% to 25% in adolescents and young adults.[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

132417</a>

1325-<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

132619</a>

1327] A high incidence of mixed-cellularity histology in childhood Hodgkin lymphoma is seen in low-income countries, and these cases are generally EBV positive (approximately 80%).[<a href="#cit/section_1.20">

132820</a>

1329]</li>

1330</ul>

1331</div>

1332<p id="_618" tabindex="-1">

1333EBV serologic status is not a prognostic factor for failure-free survival in young adult patients with Hodgkin lymphoma,[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

133417</a>

1335-<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

133619</a>

1337,<a href="#cit/section_1.21">

133821</a>

1339] but plasma EBV DNA has been associated with an inferior outcome in adults.[<a href="#cit/section_1.22">

134022</a>

1341] However, children with intermediate-risk disease with higher levels of EBV DNA at diagnosis have better outcomes.[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

134216</a>

1343] This also correlates with better outcomes for patients with mixed-cellularity disease treated with dose-dense chemotherapy (ABVE-PC [doxorubicin, bleomycin, vincristine, etoposide, prednisone, and cyclophosphamide]). Patients with a previous history of serologically confirmed infectious mononucleosis have a fourfold increased risk of developing EBV-positive Hodgkin lymphoma. These patients are not at increased risk of developing EBV-negative Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.23">

134423</a>

1345]</p>

1346</section>

1347<section id="_619">

1348<h4 id="_619_toc">

1349Immunodeficiency and Hodgkin lymphoma</h4>

1350<p id="_620" tabindex="-1">

1351Individuals with immunodeficiency have an increased risk of Hodgkin lymphoma,[<a href="#cit/section_1.24">

135224</a>

1353] although the risk of non-Hodgkin lymphoma is even higher. </p>

1354<p id="_707" tabindex="-1">

1355Characteristics of Hodgkin lymphoma presenting in the context of immunodeficiency are as follows:</p>

1356<div class="pdq-content-list">

1357<ul id="_699">

1358<li>

1359Hodgkin lymphoma usually occurs at a younger age and with histologies other than nodular sclerosing in patients with primary immunodeficiencies.[<a href="#cit/section_1.24">

136024</a>

1361]</li>

1362<li>

1363The risk of Hodgkin lymphoma increases as much as 50-fold over the general population in patients with autoimmune lymphoproliferative syndrome.[<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

136425</a>

1365]</li>

1366<li>

1367Although it is not an AIDS-defining malignancy, the incidence of Hodgkin lymphoma appears to be higher in HIV-infected individuals, including children.[<a href="#cit/section_1.26">

136826</a>

1369,<a href="#cit/section_1.27">

137027</a>

1371]</li>

1372<li>

1373Recipients of solid organ transplants who take chronic immunosuppressive medications have a higher risk of Hodgkin lymphoma than the general population.[<a href="#cit/section_1.28">

137428</a>

1375]</li>

1376<li>

1377Hodgkin lymphoma is the second most common cancer type in children who have undergone a solid organ transplant.[<a href="#cit/section_1.29">

137829</a>

1379]</li>

1380</ul>

1381</div>

1382</section>

1383</section>

1384<section id="_422">

1385<h3 id="_422_toc">

1386Clinical Presentation</h3>

1387<p id="_671" tabindex="-1">

1388The following presenting features of Hodgkin lymphoma result from direct or indirect effects of nodal or extranodal involvement and/or constitutional symptoms related to cytokine release from HRS cells and cell signaling within the tumor microenvironment:[<a href="#cit/section_1.30">

138930</a>

1390]</p>

1391<div class="pdq-content-list">

1392<ul id="_672">

1393<li>

1394Approximately 80% of patients present with painless adenopathy, most commonly involving the supraclavicular or cervical area.</li>

1395<li>

1396Mediastinal disease is present in about 75% of adolescents and young adults and may be asymptomatic. In contrast, only about 35% of young children with Hodgkin lymphoma have mediastinal involvement. This reflects the greater prevalence of mixed-cellularity and lymphocyte-predominant histology versus nodular-sclerosing histology in this age cohort.</li>

1397<li>

1398Nonspecific constitutional symptoms including fatigue, anorexia, weight loss, pruritus, night sweats, and fever occur in approximately 25% of patients.[<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

139931</a>

1400,<a href="#cit/section_1.32">

140132</a>

1402]</li>

1403<li>

1404Three specific constitutional symptoms (B symptoms) that have been correlated with prognosis are commonly used to assign risk in clinical trials. These symptoms include unexplained fever (temperature above 38.0&#176;C orally), unexplained weight loss (10% of body weight within the 6 months preceding diagnosis), and drenching night sweats.[<a href="#cit/section_1.33">

140533</a>

1406]</li>

1407<li>

1408 Female patients with large mediastinal masses and B symptoms are most likely to present with pericardial effusions.[<a href="#cit/section_1.34">

140934</a>

1410][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1411Level of evidence C1</a>

1412]</li>

1413</ul>

1414</div>

1415<p id="_1830" tabindex="-1">

1416A total of 15% to 20% of patients have noncontiguous extranodal involvement (stage IV). The most common sites of extranodal involvement are the lungs, liver, bones, and bone marrow.[<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

141731</a>

1418,<a href="#cit/section_1.32">

141932</a>

1420]</p>

1421</section>

1422<section id="_548">

1423<h3 id="_548_toc">

1424Prognostic Factors</h3>

1425<p id="_549" tabindex="-1">

1426As the treatment of Hodgkin lymphoma improved, factors associated with outcome became more difficult to identify. However, several factors continue to influence the success and choice of therapy. These factors are interrelated in the sense that disease stage, bulk, and biological aggressiveness are frequently collinear. </p>

1427<section id="_1831">

1428<h4 id="_1831_toc">

1429Pretreatment factors</h4>

1430<p id="_1832" tabindex="-1">

1431Pretreatment factors associated with an adverse outcome include the following:</p>

1432<div class="pdq-content-list">

1433<ul id="_1833">

1434<li>

1435Advanced stage of disease.[<a href="#cit/section_1.35">

143635</a>

1437,<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

143836</a>

1439]</li>

1440<li>

1441Presence of B symptoms.[<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

144231</a>

1443,<a href="#cit/section_1.32">

144432</a>

1445,<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

144636</a>

1447]</li>

1448<li>

1449Presence of bulky disease.[<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

145031</a>

1451,<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

145236</a>

1453]</li>

1454<li>

1455Presence of a pericardial effusion.[<a href="#cit/section_1.34">

145634</a>

1457][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1458Level of evidence C1</a>

1459]</li>

1460<li>

1461Presence of a pleural effusion.[<a href="#cit/section_1.37">

146237</a>

1463][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1464Level of evidence B4</a>

1465]</li>

1466<li>

1467Elevated erythrocyte sedimentation rate.[<a href="#cit/section_1.38">

146838</a>

1469]</li>

1470<li>

1471Leukocytosis (white blood cell count of 11,500/mm<span class="sup">

14723</span>

1473 or higher).[<a href="#cit/section_1.35">

147435</a>

1475]</li>

1476<li>

1477Anemia (hemoglobin lower than 11.0 g/dL).[<a href="#cit/section_1.35">

147835</a>

1479]</li>

1480<li>

1481Hypoalbuminemia.[<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

148236</a>

1483]</li>

1484<li>

1485Male sex.[<a href="#cit/section_1.32">

148632</a>

1487,<a href="#cit/section_1.35">

148835</a>

1489]</li>

1490<li>

1491Response to initial treatment with chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.39">

149239</a>

1493,<a href="#cit/section_1.40">

149440</a>

1495]</li>

1496</ul>

1497</div>

1498<p id="_1834" tabindex="-1">

1499Prognostic factors identified in select multi-institutional studies include the following:</p>

1500<div class="pdq-content-list">

1501<ul id="_1835">

1502<li>

1503In the Gesellschaft f&#252;r P&#228;diatrische Onkologie und H&#228;matologie (GPOH)-95 study, B symptoms, histology, and male sex were adverse prognostic factors for EFS on multivariate analysis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.32">

150432</a>

1505]</li>

1506<li>

1507In 320 children with clinically staged Hodgkin lymphoma treated in the Stanford-St. Jude-Dana Farber Cancer Institute consortium, male sex; stage IIB, IIIB, or IV disease; white blood cell count of 11,500/mm<span class="sup">

15083</span>

1509 or higher; and hemoglobin lower than 11.0 g/dL were significant prognostic factors for inferior disease-free survival and overall survival (OS). Prognosis was also associated with the number of adverse factors.[<a href="#cit/section_1.35">

151035</a>

1511]</li>

1512<li>

1513In the CCG-5942 study, the combination of B symptoms and bulky disease was associated with an inferior outcome.[<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

151431</a>

1515]</li>

1516<li>

1517Factors associated with adverse outcome, many of which are collinear, were evaluated by multivariable analysis in the Children's Oncology Group (COG) <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00025259">

1518AHOD0031 (NCT00025259)</a>

1519 trial for 1,734 children with intermediate-risk Hodgkin lymphoma. The most robust predictors of outcome in this homogeneously treated cohort were stage IV disease, fever, a large mediastinal mass, and low albumin (&lt;3.4 g/dL). The Childhood Hodgkin International Prognostic Score, highly predictive of EFS, was derived by giving a point for each adverse factor;[<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

152036</a>

1521] however, it requires further prospective validation.</li>

1522<li>

1523Pleural effusions have been shown to be an adverse prognostic finding in patients treated for low-stage Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.37">

152437</a>

1525][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1526Level of evidence B4</a>

1527] The risk of relapse was 25% in patients with an effusion, compared with less than 15% in patients without an effusion. Patients with effusions were more often older (15 years vs. 14 years) and had nodular-sclerosing histology.</li>

1528</ul>

1529</div>

1530<p id="_1836" tabindex="-1">

1531A single-institution study showed that Black patients had a higher relapse rate than did White patients, but OS was similar.[<a href="#cit/section_1.41">

153241</a>

1533] A COG analysis showed no difference in EFS by race or ethnicity. However, compared with non-Hispanic White children, Hispanic and non-Hispanic Black children had an inferior OS because of an increased postrelapse mortality rate.[<a href="#cit/section_1.42">

153442</a>

1535][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1536Level of evidence A1</a>

1537]</p>

1538</section>

1539<section id="_1837">

1540<h4 id="_1837_toc">

1541Response to initial chemotherapy</h4>

1542<p id="_1838" tabindex="-1">

1543The rapidity of response to initial cycles of chemotherapy also appears to be prognostically important.[<a href="#cit/section_1.39">

154439</a>

1545,<a href="#cit/section_1.40">

154640</a>

1547,<a href="#cit/section_1.43">

154843</a>

1549] Response evaluation in previous generations of trials relied on computed tomography and gallium uptake; positron emission tomography (PET) scanning is now routinely used to assess early response in pediatric Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.44">

155044</a>

1551] Fluorine F 18-fludeoxyglucose PET avidity after two cycles of chemotherapy for Hodgkin lymphoma in adults has been shown to predict treatment failure and progression-free survival.[<a href="#cit/section_1.45">

155245</a>

1553-<a href="#cit/section_1.47">

155447</a>

1555] Reduction in PET avidity after one cycle of chemotherapy was associated with a favorable EFS outcome in children with limited-stage classical Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.38">

155638</a>

1557] Additional studies in children are ongoing to assess the role of early

1558PET-based response in modifying therapy and predicting outcome.</p>

1559<p id="_1839" tabindex="-1">

1560Prognostic factors will continue to change because of risk stratification and choice of therapy, with parameters such as disease stage, bulk, systemic symptomatology, and early response to chemotherapy used to stratify therapeutic assignment.</p>

1561</section>

1562</section>

Blood 109 (3): 905-9, 2007.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=17018856&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=17018856&amp;dopt=Abstract">

1752[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

1753</li>

1754</ol>

1755</div>

1756

1757 </section>

1758

1759

1760

1761 <section id="_11">

1762

1763 <h2>

1764Cellular Classification and Biological Correlates of Childhood Hodgkin Lymphoma</h2>

1765

1766 <div id="_section_11" class="pdq-sections">

1767<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

1768<h6>

1769In This Section</h6>

1770<ul>

1771<li>

1772<a href="#_169_toc">

1773Classical Hodgkin Lymphoma</a>

1774</li>

1775<li>

1776<a href="#_176_toc">

1777Nodular Lymphocyte-Predominant Hodgkin Lymphoma</a>

1778</li>

1779</ul>

1780</nav>

1781<p id="_607" tabindex="-1">

1782Hodgkin lymphoma is characterized by a variable number of characteristic multinucleated giant cells (Hodgkin and Reed-Sternberg [HRS] cells) or large mononuclear cell variants (lymphocytic and histiocytic cells). These cells are in a background of inflammatory cells consisting of small lymphocytes, histiocytes, epithelioid histiocytes, neutrophils, eosinophils, plasma cells, and fibroblasts. The inflammatory cells are present in different proportions depending on the histological subtype. It has been conclusively shown that HRS cells and/or lymphocytic and histiocytic cells represent a clonal population. Almost all cases of Hodgkin lymphoma arise from germinal center B cells.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

17831</a>

1784-<a href="#cit/section_2.3">

17853</a>

1786]</p>

1787<p id="_1840" tabindex="-1">

1788 The histological features and clinical symptoms of Hodgkin lymphoma have been attributed to the numerous cytokines, chemokines, and products of the tumor necrosis factor receptors family secreted by the HRS cells and cell signaling within the tumor microenvironment.[<a href="#cit/section_2.4">

17894</a>

1790-<a href="#cit/section_2.6">

17916</a>

1792]</p>

1793<p id="_700" tabindex="-1">

1794The hallmark of Hodgkin lymphoma is the HRS cell and its variants,[<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

17957</a>

1796] which have the following features:</p>

1797<div class="pdq-content-list">

1798<ul id="_701">

1799<li>

1800The HRS cell is a binucleated or multinucleated giant cell with a bilobed nucleus and two large nucleoli that give a characteristic owl's eye appearance.[<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

18017</a>

1802]</li>

1803<li>

1804The malignant HRS cell comprises only about 1% of the abundant reactive cellular infiltrate of lymphocytes, macrophages, granulocytes, and eosinophils in involved specimens.[<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

18057</a>

1806]</li>

1807<li>

1808HRS cells almost always express CD30. Approximately 70% of patients express CD15, and 6% to 10% of patients express CD20. Generally, HRS cells do not express B-cell antigens such as CD45, CD19, and CD79A.[<a href="#cit/section_2.8">

18098</a>

1810-<a href="#cit/section_2.10">

181110</a>

1812]</li>

1813<li>

1814In nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma, the malignant cells equivalent to HRS cells are lymphocyte-predominant (LP) cells, previously named lymphocyte and histiocytic (L&amp;H) cells and sometimes referred to as popcorn cells. They are usually mononuclear, with a markedly convoluted and lobated nucleus (hence popcorn cells). LP cells do not express CD30, but they do express CD20 and other B-cell surface antigens. This evidence shows that nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma is biologically distinct from other subtypes of Hodgkin lymphoma.</li>

1815</ul>

1816</div>

1817<p id="_167" tabindex="-1">

1818Hodgkin lymphoma can be divided into the following two broad pathological classes:[<a href="#cit/section_2.11">

181911</a>

1820,<a href="#cit/section_2.12">

182112</a>

1822]</p>

1823<div class="pdq-content-list">

1824<ul id="_168">

1825<li>

1826<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_169">

1827Classical Hodgkin lymphoma</a>

1828.</li>

1829<li>

1830<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_176">

1831Nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma</a>

1832.</li>

1833</ul>

1834</div>

1835<section id="_169">

1836<h3 id="_169_toc">

1837Classical Hodgkin Lymphoma</h3>

1838<p id="_170" tabindex="-1">

1839Classical Hodgkin lymphoma is divided into four subtypes. These subtypes are defined according to the number of HRS cells, characteristics of the inflammatory milieu, and the presence or absence of fibrosis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.3">

18403</a>

1841]</p>

1842<p id="_702" tabindex="-1">

1843Characteristics of the four histological subtypes of classical Hodgkin lymphoma include the following:</p>

1844<div class="pdq-content-list">

1845<ul id="_175">

1846<li>

1847<strong>

1848Nodular-sclerosing Hodgkin lymphoma. </strong>

1849This histology accounts for approximately 80% of Hodgkin lymphoma cases in older children and adolescents but only 55% of cases in younger children in the United States.[<a href="#cit/section_2.13">

185013</a>

1851] <p id="_1841" tabindex="-1">

1852 This subtype is distinguished by the presence of collagenous bands that divide the lymph node into nodules, which often contain an HRS cell variant called the lacunar cell. Transforming growth factor-beta may be responsible for the fibrosis in this subtype.</p>

1853<p id="_717" tabindex="-1">

1854A study of over 600 patients with nodular-sclerosing Hodgkin lymphoma from three university hospitals in the United States showed that two haplotypes in the HLA class II region correlated with a 70% increased risk of developing nodular-sclerosing Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.14">

185514</a>

1856] Another haplotype was associated with a 60% decreased risk of developing Hodgkin lymphoma. These haplotypes are thought to be associated with atypical immune responses that predispose patients to Hodgkin lymphoma.</p>

1857</li>

1858<li>

1859<strong>

1860Mixed-cellularity Hodgkin lymphoma. </strong>

1861This subtype is more common in young children than in adolescents and adults, accounting for approximately 20% of cases in children younger than 10 years, but only approximately 9% of cases in older children and adolescents aged 10 to 19 years in the United States.[<a href="#cit/section_2.13">

186213</a>

1863] A high percentage of mixed-cellularity Hodgkin lymphoma cases are Epstein-Barr virus positive.[<a href="#cit/section_2.15">

186415</a>

1865]<p id="_1842" tabindex="-1">

1866HRS cells are frequent in a background of abundant normal reactive cells (lymphocytes, plasma cells, eosinophils, and histiocytes). Interleukin-5 may be responsible for the eosinophilia in mixed-cellularity Hodgkin lymphoma. This subtype can be difficult to distinguish from non-Hodgkin lymphoma. </p>

1867</li>

1868<li>

1869<strong>

1870Lymphocyte-rich Hodgkin lymphoma. </strong>

1871This subtype may have a nodular appearance, but immunophenotypical analysis shows a distinction between this form of Hodgkin lymphoma and nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.16">

187216</a>

1873] Lymphocyte-rich classical Hodgkin lymphoma cells express CD15 and CD30. </li>

1874<li>

1875<strong>

1876Lymphocyte-depleted Hodgkin lymphoma. </strong>

1877This subtype is rare in children. It is common in adult patients with HIV.<p id="_1843" tabindex="-1">

1878 This subtype is characterized by numerous large, bizarre malignant cells, many HRS cells, and few lymphocytes. Diffuse fibrosis and necrosis are common. Many cases previously diagnosed as lymphocyte-depleted Hodgkin lymphoma are now recognized as diffuse large B-cell lymphoma, anaplastic large cell lymphoma, or nodular-sclerosing classical Hodgkin lymphoma with lymphocyte depletion.[<a href="#cit/section_2.17">

187917</a>

1880]</p>

1881</li>

1882</ul>

1883</div>

1884</section>

1885<section id="_176">

1886<h3 id="_176_toc">

1887Nodular Lymphocyte-Predominant Hodgkin Lymphoma</h3>

1888<p id="_852" tabindex="-1">

1889The frequency of nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma in the pediatric population ranges from 5% to 10% in different studies, with a higher frequency in children younger than 10 years than in children aged 10 to 19 years.[<a href="#cit/section_2.13">

189013</a>

1891] This type of Hodgkin lymphoma is most common in males younger than 18 years.[<a href="#cit/section_2.18">

189218</a>

1893,<a href="#cit/section_2.19">

189419</a>

1895]</p>

1896<p id="_853" tabindex="-1">

1897Characteristics of nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma include the following:</p>

1898<div class="pdq-content-list">

1899<ul id="_722">

1900<li>

1901Patients generally present with localized, nonbulky, peripheral lymphadenopathy that rarely involves the mediastinum.[<a href="#cit/section_2.18">

190218</a>

1903,<a href="#cit/section_2.19">

190419</a>

1905] Less than 10% of patients have systemic B symptoms, although some patients with involved lymph nodes, especially cervical, may experience discomfort.[<a href="#cit/section_2.20">

190620</a>

1907] </li>

1908<li>

1909Nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma is characterized by molecular and immunophenotypical evidence of B-lineage differentiation with the following distinctive features:<div class="pdq-content-list">

1910<ul id="_723" class="list-dash">

1911<li>

1912Large cells with multilobed nuclei, termed LP cells (previously referred to as L&amp;H cells and sometimes referred to as popcorn cells), express B-cell antigens such as CD19, CD20, CD22, and CD79A. They are negative for CD15 and may or may not express CD30.[<a href="#cit/section_2.21">

191321</a>

1914] They also express the B-cell transcription factors <em class="gene-name">

1915OCT2</em>

1916 and <em class="gene-name">

1917BOB1</em>

1918.[<a href="#cit/section_2.22">

191922</a>

1920]</li>

1921<li>

1922Reliable discrimination from non-Hodgkin lymphoma is problematic in diffuse subtypes with lymphocytic and histiocytic cells set against a diffuse background of reactive T cells.[<a href="#cit/section_2.23">

192323</a>

1924] </li>

1925<li>

1926Nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma can be difficult to distinguish from progressive transformation of germinal centers and/or T-cell&#8211;rich B-cell lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.24">

192724</a>

1928]</li>

1929<li>

1930Histological variants may impact event-free survival (EFS).[<a href="#cit/section_2.25">

193125</a>

1932]</li>

1933<li>

1934Immunoglobulin (Ig) D expression connotes a distinct type of nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma that is associated with a very high male-to-female ratio (&gt;10:1).[<a href="#cit/section_2.26">

193526</a>

1936,<a href="#cit/section_2.27">

193727</a>

1938] In one study, 87 of the 124 pediatric cases (70%) versus 32 of the 84 adult (&gt;18 years) cases (38%) tested expressed IgD in LP cells (<em>

1939P</em>

1940 &lt; .0001). The median age of the IgD-positive patients was 14 years.[<a href="#cit/section_2.26">

194126</a>

1942] In a second study, the median age of IgD-positive patients was 21 years, compared with a median age of 44 years for the IgD-negative patients.[<a href="#cit/section_2.27">

194327</a>

1944] The IgD-positive patients were more likely to present with cervical node involvement (58%) than were the IgD-negative patients (18%). IgD expression was not associated with EFS.</li>

1945</ul>

1946</div>

1947</li>

1948<li>

1949Pediatric patients (aged &lt;20 years) have better outcomes than do adult patients, even when controlled for other prognostic factors.[<a href="#cit/section_2.19">

195019</a>

1951] Chemotherapy and/or radiation therapy produce excellent long-term progression-free survival and overall survival in patients with nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma. However, radiation therapy alone should not be considered for prepubescent patients because the evidence-based doses necessary for tumor control are associated with musculoskeletal impairment. When radiation is administered with chemotherapy, lower radiation doses are effective. Late recurrences have been reported up to 10 years after initial therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_2.20">

195220</a>

1953,<a href="#cit/section_2.28">

195428</a>

1955,<a href="#cit/section_2.29">

195629</a>

1957]; [<a href="#cit/section_2.30">

195830</a>

1959][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1960Level of evidence B4</a>

1961]</li>

1962<li>

1963Deaths of individuals with nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma are more frequently related to treatment complications and/or the development of subsequent neoplasms (including non-Hodgkin lymphoma) than in refractory disease. This finding underscores the importance of judicious use of chemotherapy and radiation therapy at initial presentation and after recurrent disease.[<a href="#cit/section_2.28">

196428</a>

1965,<a href="#cit/section_2.29">

196629</a>

1967]</li>

1968</ul>

1969</div>

2101 <section id="_2018">

2102

2103 <h2>

2104Genomics of Hodgkin Lymphoma</h2>

2105

2106 <div id="_section_2018" class="pdq-sections">

2107<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2108<h6>

2109In This Section</h6>

2110<ul>

2111<li>

2112<a href="#_sm_CDR0000806139_2034_toc">

2113Genomics of Classical Hodgkin Lymphoma</a>

2114</li>

2115<li>

2116<a href="#_sm_CDR0000806139_2035_toc">

2117Genomics of Nodular Lymphocyte-Predominant Hodgkin Lymphoma</a>

2118</li>

2119</ul>

2120</nav>

2121<section id="_sm_CDR0000806139_2034">

2122<h3 id="_sm_CDR0000806139_2034_toc">

2123Genomics of Classical Hodgkin Lymphoma</h3>

2124<p id="_sm_CDR0000806139_2019" tabindex="-1">

2125Classical Hodgkin lymphoma has a gene expression and mutation profile that differs from that of other lymphomas. The exception is primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma, which shares many genomic and cytogenetic characteristics with Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

21261</a>

2127,<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

21282</a>

2129] Characterization of genomic alterations for Hodgkin lymphoma is challenging because malignant Hodgkin and Reed-Sternberg (HRS) cells make up only a small percentage of the overall tumor mass. Because of this finding, special methods, such as microdissection of HRS cells or flow cytometry cell sorting, are required before applying molecular analysis methods.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

21302</a>

2131,<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

21323</a>

2133] Hodgkin lymphoma genomic alterations can also be assessed using special sequencing methods applied to circulating cell-free DNA (cfDNA) in peripheral blood of patients with Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

21344</a>

2135]</p>

2136<p id="_sm_CDR0000806139_2020" tabindex="-1">

2137The genomic alterations observed in Hodgkin lymphoma fall into several categories, including immune evasion alterations, JAK-STAT pathway alterations, alterations leading to nuclear factor kappa-light-chain-enhancer of activated B cells (NF-kappaB) activation, and others: </p>

2138<div class="pdq-content-list">

2139<ul id="_sm_CDR0000806139_2021">

2140<li>

2141Multiple genomic alterations contribute to immune evasion in Hodgkin lymphoma.<div class="pdq-content-list">

2142<ul id="_sm_CDR0000806139_2022">

2143<li>

2144Copy number gain or amplification at chromosome 9p24 is observed in most cases of Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

21455</a>

2146,<a href="#cit/section_3.6">

21476</a>

2148] This region encodes the immune checkpoint genes <em class="gene-name">

2149CD274</em>

2150 (encoding PD-L1) and <em class="gene-name">

2151PDCD1LG2</em>

2152 (encoding PD-L2). These genomic alterations lead to increased expression of these checkpoint proteins.[<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

21535</a>

2154,<a href="#cit/section_3.6">

21556</a>

2156]</li>

2157<li>

2158Gene fusions involving <em class="gene-name">

2159CIITA</em>

2160, which is the master transcriptional regulator of major histocompatibility complex (MHC) class II expression, were reported in 15% of Hodgkin lymphoma cases.[<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

21617</a>

2162] Similar alterations are found in primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma, and they lead to decreased CIITA protein expression and loss of MHC class II expression.[<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

21637</a>

2164,<a href="#cit/section_3.8">

21658</a>

2166]</li>

2167<li>

2168Beta-2-microglobulin (the invariant chain of the MHC class I) frequently shows decreased/absent expression in HRS cells, with accompanying decreased MHC class I expression.[<a href="#cit/section_3.9">

21699</a>

2170] Inactivating mutations in <em class="gene-name">

2171B2M</em>

2172, the gene that encodes beta-2-microglobulin, are common in Hodgkin lymphoma and lead to reduced expression of MHC class I.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

21732</a>

2174,<a href="#cit/section_3.10">

217510</a>

2176] Inactivating mutations in <em class="gene-name">

2177B2M</em>

2178 occur more frequently in Epstein-Barr virus (EBV)-negative Hodgkin lymphoma than in EBV-positive Hodgkin lymphoma,[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

21792</a>

2180] which explains the higher rates of beta-2 microglobulin and MHC class I expression for EBV-positive Hodgkin lymphoma, compared with EBV-negative Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.9">

21819</a>

2182]</li>

2183</ul>

2184</div>

2185</li>

2186<li>

2187Genomic alterations involving genes in the JAK-STAT pathway are observed in most cases of Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

21883</a>

2189] Genes in the JAK-STAT pathway for which genomic alterations are reported include: <div class="pdq-content-list">

2190<ul id="_sm_CDR0000806139_2023">

2191<li>

2192<em class="gene-name">

2193SOCS1</em>

2194, a negative regulator of JAK-STAT signaling, is inactivated by mutations in 60% to 70% of Hodgkin lymphoma cases.[<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

21953</a>

2196] In a study of pediatric Hodgkin lymphoma using cfDNA collected before treatment, <em class="gene-name">

2197SOCS1</em>

2198 was the most frequently mutated gene, with mutations in 60% of all cases and approximately 80% of cases in which genomic alterations were detected in cfDNA.[<a href="#cit/section_3.11">

219911</a>

2200]</li>

2201<li>

2202Activating <em class="gene-name">

2203STAT6</em>

2204 mutations occurring at hot spots in the DNA-binding domain are observed in approximately 30% of Hodgkin lymphoma cases.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

22052</a>

2206,<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

22073</a>

2208]</li>

2209<li>

2210The chromosome 9p region that contains <em class="gene-name">

2211CD274</em>

2212 and <em class="gene-name">

2213PDCD1LG2</em>

2214, which shows gains and amplifications in Hodgkin lymphoma, also contains <em class="gene-name">

2215JAK2</em>

2216.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

22172</a>

2218,<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

22193</a>

2220,<a href="#cit/section_3.12">

222112</a>

2222] Chromosome 9p gain/amplification is thought to further augment JAK-STAT pathway signaling.[<a href="#cit/section_3.12">

222312</a>

2224]</li>

2225<li>

2226Inactivating mutations in <em class="gene-name">

2227PTPN1</em>

2228, a phosphatase that inhibits JAK-STAT pathway signaling, were observed in approximately 20% of Hodgkin lymphoma cases.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

22292</a>

2230,<a href="#cit/section_3.13">

223113</a>

2232]</li>

2233<li>

2234Mutations in other genes affecting JAK-STAT pathway signaling have also been reported, including <em class="gene-name">

2235JAK1</em>

2236, <em class="gene-name">

2237STAT3</em>

2238, <em class="gene-name">

2239STAT5B</em>

2240, and <em class="gene-name">

2241CSF2RB</em>

2242.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

22432</a>

2244,<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

22453</a>

2246]</li>

2247</ul>

2248</div>

2249</li>

2250<li>

2251Genomic alterations leading to NF-kappaB activation are also common in Hodgkin lymphoma. <div class="pdq-content-list">

2252<ul id="_sm_CDR0000806139_2024">

2253<li>

2254The <em class="gene-name">

2255REL</em>

2256 gene at chromosome 2p16.1 shows genomic gain or amplification in approximately one-third of Hodgkin lymphoma cases.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

22572</a>

2258,<a href="#cit/section_3.14">

225914</a>

2260]</li>

2261<li>

2262EBV-positive Hodgkin lymphoma expresses the EBV latent membrane protein 1 (LMP1) at the cell surface. This protein acts like a constitutively activated receptor of the TNF receptor family to cause activation of the NF-kappaB pathway.[<a href="#cit/section_3.15">

226315</a>

2264]</li>

2265<li>

2266Inactivating mutations in genes that inhibit NF-kappaB pathway signaling, including <em class="gene-name">

2267TNFAIP3</em>

2268, <em class="gene-name">

2269NFKBIA</em>

2270, and <em class="gene-name">

2271NFKBIE</em>

2272, are common in Hodgkin lymphoma. Inactivation of the gene products for these genes leads to NF-kappaB pathway activation. <em class="gene-name">

2273TNFAIP3</em>

2274 is the most commonly altered inhibitor of NF-kappaB pathway signaling, and loss of function alterations occur by either mutations or by focal 6q23.3 or arm-level 6q loss.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

22752</a>

2276,<a href="#cit/section_3.16">

227716</a>

2278] <em class="gene-name">

2279TNFAIP3</em>

2280 genomic alterations are much more common in EBV-negative Hodgkin lymphoma than in EBV-positive Hodgkin lymphoma, suggesting that LMP1 expression in EBV-positive Hodgkin lymphoma obviates the need for TNFAIP3 loss of function.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

22812</a>

2282,<a href="#cit/section_3.16">

228316</a>

2284]</li>

2285</ul>

2286</div>

2287</li>

2288<li>

2289Other genes that are mutated in Hodgkin lymphoma include <em class="gene-name">

2290XPO1</em>

2291, <em class="gene-name">

2292RBM38</em>

2293, <em class="gene-name">

2294ACTB</em>

2295, <em class="gene-name">

2296ARID1A</em>

2297, and <em class="gene-name">

2298GNA13</em>

2299.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

23002</a>

2301-<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

23024</a>

2303]</li>

2304<li>

2305Hodgkin lymphoma is derived from a B-cell progenitor, and HRS cells generally do not express B-cell surface antigens. HRS cells do have immunoglobulin (Ig) heavy and light chain V gene rearrangements typical of B cells.[<a href="#cit/section_3.17">

230617</a>

2307,<a href="#cit/section_3.18">

230818</a>

2309] Although Ig genes have undergone rearrangements in HRS cells, the rearrangements are nonproductive and B-cell receptor is not expressed. </li>

2310</ul>

2311</div>

2312</section>

2313<section id="_sm_CDR0000806139_2035">

2314<h3 id="_sm_CDR0000806139_2035_toc">

2315Genomics of Nodular Lymphocyte-Predominant Hodgkin Lymphoma</h3>

2316<p id="_sm_CDR0000806139_2025" tabindex="-1">

2317The lymphocyte-predominant (LP) cells of nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma have distinctive genomic characteristics compared with the HRS cells of Hodgkin lymphoma. As with Hodgkin lymphoma, genomic characterization is complicated by the low percentage of malignant cells within a tumor mass. </p>

2318<div class="pdq-content-list">

2319<ul id="_sm_CDR0000806139_2026">

2320<li>

2321LP cells express B-cell antigens (e.g., CD19, CD20, CD22, and CD79A) and B-cell transcription factors (e.g., OCT2 and BOB1).[<a href="#cit/section_3.19">

232219</a>

2323,<a href="#cit/section_3.20">

232420</a>

2325]</li>

2326<li>

2327The expression of Bcl-6 and the presence of somatic hypermutations in the variable region of rearranged Ig heavy chain genes point to a germinal center derivation for LP cells.[<a href="#cit/section_3.21">

232821</a>

2329,<a href="#cit/section_3.22">

233022</a>

2331]</li>

2332<li>

2333IgD expression connotes a distinct type of nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma that is associated with a very high male-to-female ratio (&gt;10:1).[<a href="#cit/section_3.23">

233423</a>

2335,<a href="#cit/section_3.24">

233624</a>

2337] An evaluation of the antigenic specificity of the B-cell receptor in cases of IgD-positive nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma found that in 7 of 8 cases (6 of 8 patients aged &#8804;18 years) the B-cell receptor recognized the DNA-directed RNA polymerase (RpoC) from <em>

2338Moraxella catarrhalis</em>

2339.[<a href="#cit/section_3.25">

234025</a>

2341] High-titer, light-chain-restricted anti-RpoC IgG1 serum-antibodies were observed in these patients. In addition, MID/hag is a superantigen expressed by <em>

2342M. catarrhalis</em>

2343 that binds to the Fc domain of IgD and activates IgD-positive B cells. These observations support a role for <em>

2344M. catarrhalis</em>

2345 in the development and maintenance of IgD-positive nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma. </li>

2346<li>

2347Genomic analysis of nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma is limited to a small number of patients using gene panels to evaluate microdissected specimens containing LP cells. Recurring mutated genes include <em class="gene-name">

2348SOCS1</em>

2349 (an inhibitor of JAK-STAT pathway signaling), <em class="gene-name">

2350DUSP2</em>

2351 (a dual specificity phosphatase that is a negative regulator of the MAP kinase pathway), <em class="gene-name">

2352JUNB</em>

2353 (a transcription factor in the activator protein-1 family), and <em class="gene-name">

2354SGK1</em>

2355 (a serine-threonine kinase).[<a href="#cit/section_3.26">

235626</a>

2357-<a href="#cit/section_3.28">

235828</a>

2359]</li>

2360</ul>

2361</div>

2362</section>

2485 <section id="_15">

2486

2487 <h2>

2488Diagnosis and Staging Information for Childhood Hodgkin Lymphoma</h2>

2489

2490 <div id="_section_15" class="pdq-sections">

2491<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2492<h6>

2493In This Section</h6>

2494<ul>

2495<li>

2496<a href="#_425_toc">

2497Diagnostic and Staging Evaluation<ul>

2498<li>

2499<a href="#_627_toc">

2500Systemic symptoms</a>

2501</li>

2502<li>

2503<a href="#_632_toc">

2504Physical examination</a>

2505</li>

2506<li>

2507<a href="#_634_toc">

2508Laboratory studies</a>

2509</li>

2510<li>

2511<a href="#_636_toc">

2512Anatomical imaging</a>

2513</li>

2514<li>

2515<a href="#_643_toc">

2516Functional imaging</a>

2517</li>

2518</ul>

2519</a>

2520</li>

2521<li>

2522<a href="#_647_toc">

2523Establishing the Diagnosis of Hodgkin Lymphoma</a>

2524</li>

2525<li>

2526<a href="#_650_toc">

2527Lugano Staging Classification for Hodgkin Lymphoma</a>

2528</li>

2529<li>

2530<a href="#_30_toc">

2531Risk Stratification</a>

2532</li>

2533<li>

2534<a href="#_519_toc">

2535Response Assessment<ul>

2536<li>

2537<a href="#_521_toc">

2538Interim response assessment</a>

2539</li>

2540<li>

2545</a>

2546</li>

2547</ul>

2548</nav>

2549<p id="_424" tabindex="-1">

2550Staging and evaluation of disease status is undertaken at diagnosis, early in the course of chemotherapy, and at the end of chemotherapy. </p>

2551<section id="_425">

2552<h3 id="_425_toc">

2553Diagnostic and Staging Evaluation</h3>

2554<p id="_184" tabindex="-1">

2555The diagnostic and staging evaluation is critical for the selection of treatment. Initial evaluation of the child with Hodgkin lymphoma includes the following:</p>

2556<div class="pdq-content-list">

2557<ul id="_510">

2558<li>

2559History of systemic symptoms.</li>

2560<li>

2561Physical examination.</li>

2562<li>

2563Laboratory studies, including complete blood count, chemistry panel with albumin, and erythrocyte sedimentation rate.</li>

2564<li>

2565Anatomical imaging, including chest x-ray and computed tomography (CT) or magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of the neck, chest, abdomen, and pelvis. MRI has the advantage of limiting radiation exposure.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

25661</a>

2567,<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

25682</a>

2569]</li>

2570<li>

2571Functional imaging, including positron emission tomography (PET)-CT or PET-MRI.[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

25722</a>

2573] </li>

2574</ul>

2575</div>

2576<section id="_627">

2577<h4 id="_627_toc">

2578Systemic symptoms</h4>

2579<p id="_628" tabindex="-1">

2580The following three constitutional symptoms (B symptoms) correlate with prognosis and are used in assignment of stage:</p>

2581<div class="pdq-content-list">

2582<ul id="_629">

2583<li>

2584Unexplained fever with temperatures above 38.0&#176;C orally.</li>

2585<li>

2586Unexplained weight loss of 10% within the 6 months preceding diagnosis.</li>

2587<li>

2588Drenching night sweats.</li>

2589</ul>

2590</div>

2591<p id="_630" tabindex="-1">

2592Additional Hodgkin-associated constitutional symptoms that lack prognostic significance include the following:</p>

2593<div class="pdq-content-list">

2594<ul id="_631">

2595<li>

2596Pruritus.</li>

2597<li>

2598Alcohol-induced nodal pain.</li>

2599</ul>

2600</div>

2601</section>

2602<section id="_632">

2603<h4 id="_632_toc">

2604Physical examination</h4>

2605<div class="pdq-content-list">

2606<ul id="_633">

2607<li>

2608All node-bearing areas, including the Waldeyer ring, should be assessed by careful physical examination.</li>

2609<li>

2610Enlarged nodes should be measured to establish a baseline for evaluation of therapy response.</li>

2611</ul>

2612</div>

2613</section>

2614<section id="_634">

2615<h4 id="_634_toc">

2616Laboratory studies</h4>

2617<div class="pdq-content-list">

2618<ul id="_635">

2619<li>

2620Hematological and chemical blood parameters (e.g., albumin) show nonspecific changes that may correlate with disease extent.</li>

2621<li>

2622Abnormalities of peripheral blood counts may include neutrophilic leukocytosis, lymphopenia, eosinophilia, and monocytosis.</li>

2623<li>

2624Acute-phase reactants such as the erythrocyte sedimentation rate and C-reactive protein, if abnormal at diagnosis, may be useful in follow-up evaluation.[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

26253</a>

2626]</li>

2627</ul>

2628</div>

2629</section>

2630<section id="_636">

2631<h4 id="_636_toc">

2632Anatomical imaging</h4>

2633<p id="_637" tabindex="-1">

2634Anatomical information from CT or MRI is complemented by PET functional imaging, which is sensitive in determining initial sites of involvement, particularly in sites too small to be considered clearly involved by CT or MRI criteria. Collaboration across international groups to harmonize definitions is ongoing.[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

26352</a>

2636,<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

26374</a>

2638] Metabolic tumor volume calculations may enhance the prognostic utility of PET scans.[<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

26395</a>

2640]</p>

2641<section id="_638">

2642<h5 id="_638_toc">

2643Definition of bulky disease</h5>

2644<p id="_639" tabindex="-1">

2645Historically, the presence of bulky disease, especially mediastinal bulk, predicted an increased risk of local failure and resulted in the incorporation of bulk as an important factor in treatment assignment. The definition of bulk has varied across pediatric protocols and evolved over time with advances in diagnostic imaging technology.[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

26464</a>

2647]</p>

2648<p id="_1844" tabindex="-1">

2649The criteria for bulky mediastinal and nonmediastinal disease are as follows:</p>

2650<div class="pdq-content-list">

2651<ul id="_1845">

2652<li>

2653<strong>

2654Mediastinal. </strong>

2655In North American protocols, the posteroanterior chest radiograph remains important because the criterion for bulky mediastinal lymphadenopathy is defined by the ratio of the diameter of the mediastinal lymph node mass to the maximal diameter of the rib cage on an upright chest radiograph. A ratio of 33% or higher is considered bulky. In contrast, the EuroNet-Pediatric Hodgkin Lymphoma Group defines mediastinal bulk by the volume of the largest contiguous lymph node mass being 200 mL or more on CT. These two definitions differ from the published consensus guidelines from the International Conference on Malignant Lymphomas Imaging Group (Lugano), which defines bulk as a mass 10 cm or larger seen unidimensionally on CT.[<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

26566</a>

2657]</li>

2658<li>

2659<strong>

2660Nonmediastinal. </strong>

2661The criteria for bulky peripheral, nonmediastinal lymphadenopathy have also varied over the years in cooperative group study protocols, and this disease characteristic has not been consistently used for treatment stratification. In contemporary U.S. protocols, bulky peripheral lymphadenopathy is defined as greater than 6 cm, with aggregates measured transversely or cranial-caudal. In EuroNet protocols, peripheral adenopathy is again defined as a volume of 200 mL or more, which is generally larger than a 6-cm unidimensional mass.</li>

2662</ul>

2663</div>

2664</section>

2665<section id="_640">

2666<h5 id="_640_toc">

2667Criteria for lymphomatous involvement by CT or MRI</h5>

2668<p id="_641" tabindex="-1">

2669Defining strict CT or MRI size criteria for lymphomatous nodal involvement is complicated by several factors, such as size overlap between what proves to be benign reactive hyperplasia versus malignant lymphadenopathy, the implication of nodal clusters, and obliquity of node orientation to the scan plane. Additional difficulties more specific to children include greater variability of normal nodal size and the frequent occurrence of reactive hyperplasia. </p>

2670<p id="_709" tabindex="-1">

2671General concepts to consider for defining lymphomatous involvement by CT or MRI include the following:</p>

2672<div class="pdq-content-list">

2673<ul id="_642">

2674<li>

2675Contiguous nodal clustering or matting is highly suggestive of lymphomatous involvement.</li>

2676<li>

2677Any focal mass lesion large enough to characterize in a visceral organ is considered lymphomatous involvement unless the imaging characteristics indicate an alternative etiology. </li>

2678<li>

2679Criteria for nodal involvement may vary by cooperative group or protocol.[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

26804</a>

2681] <div class="pdq-content-list">

2682<ul id="_808" class="list-dash">

2683<li>

2684Children's Oncology Group (COG) and EuroNet protocols consider lymph nodes abnormal if the long axis is greater than 2 cm, regardless of the short axis and PET avidity. Lymph nodes with a long axis measuring between 1 cm and 2 cm are only considered abnormal if they are part of a conglomerate of nodes and are fluorine F 18-fludeoxyglucose (18F-FDG) PET positive.</li>

2685<li>

2686In the Gesellschaft f&#252;r P&#228;diatrische Onkologie und H&#228;matologie (GPOH) <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00416832">

2687GPOH-HD-2002</a>

2688 study, nodal involvement was defined as node size greater than 2 cm in largest diameter. The node was not involved if it was less than 1 cm and was considered questionable if it was between 1 cm and 2 cm. The decision on involvement was then made on the basis of additional clinical evidence.[<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

26897</a>

2690]</li>

2691<li>

2692In an analysis of 47,828 imaging measurements from 2,983 adult and pediatric patients with lymphoma enrolled in ten multicenter clinical trials, a single dimension measurement of 15 mm or more constituted involvement.[<a href="#cit/section_4.8">

26938</a>

2694]</li>

2695</ul>

2696</div>

2697</li>

2698</ul>

2699</div>

2700</section>

2701</section>

2702<section id="_643">

2703<h4 id="_643_toc">

2704Functional imaging</h4>

2705<p id="_657" tabindex="-1">

2706The recommended functional imaging procedure for initial staging is PET, using the radioactive glucose analogue, 18F-FDG.[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

27072</a>

2708,<a href="#cit/section_4.9">

27099</a>

2710,<a href="#cit/section_4.10">

271110</a>

2712] 18F-FDG PET identifies areas of tumor with increased metabolic activity, specifically anaerobic glycolysis. PET-CT, which integrates functional and anatomical tumor characteristics, is often used for staging and monitoring of pediatric patients with Hodgkin lymphoma. Residual or persistent 18F-FDG avidity has been correlated with poor prognosis and the need for additional therapy in posttreatment evaluation.[<a href="#cit/section_4.11">

271311</a>

2714-<a href="#cit/section_4.13">

271513</a>

2716]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.14">

271714</a>

2718][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2719Level of evidence B4</a>

2720] Whole-body MRI, with diffusion-weighted imaging, compares favorably to PET-CT for staging of pediatric Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.15">

272115</a>

2722]</p>

2723<p id="_644" tabindex="-1">

2724General concepts to consider for defining lymphomatous involvement by 18F-FDG PET include the following:</p>

2725<div class="pdq-content-list">

2726<ul id="_645">

2727<li>

2728Concordance between PET and CT data is generally high for nodal regions but may be significantly lower for extranodal sites. In one study analyzing pediatric patients with Hodgkin lymphoma, assessment of initial staging comparing PET and CT data demonstrated concordance of approximately 86% overall. Concordance rates were significantly lower for the spleen, lung nodules, bone, bone marrow, and pleural and pericardial effusions.[<a href="#cit/section_4.16">

272916</a>

2730] A meta-analysis of nine clinical studies showed that PET-CT achieved high sensitivity (96.9%) and high specificity (99.7%) in detecting bone marrow involvement in newly diagnosed patients with Hodgkin lymphoma. Focal involvement was highly predictive of bone marrow involvement.[<a href="#cit/section_4.17">

273117</a>

2732,<a href="#cit/section_4.18">

273318</a>

2734]</li>

2735<li>

2736Integration of data acquired from PET scans can lead to changes in staging.[<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

27376</a>

2738,<a href="#cit/section_4.19">

273919</a>

2740]</li>

2741<li>

2742Staging criteria using PET and CT

2743scan information is protocol dependent. Generally, areas of PET positivity that do not correspond to an anatomical lesion by clinical examination or CT scan size criteria should be disregarded in staging, with the possible exception of focally PET-positive bone marrow findings.</li>

2744<li>

2745A suspected anatomical lesion that is PET negative should not be considered involved unless proven by biopsy.</li>

2746</ul>

2747</div>

2748<p id="_646" tabindex="-1">

274918F-FDG PET has limitations in the pediatric setting. Tracer avidity may be seen in a variety of nonmalignant conditions, including thymic rebound commonly observed after completion of lymphoma therapy. 18F-FDG avidity in normal tissues, such as brown fat in the neck, may confound interpretation of the presence of nodal involvement by lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.9">

27509</a>

2751]</p>

2752<p id="_1812" tabindex="-1">

2753Visual PET criteria are scored according to uptake involved by lymphoma from the Deauville 5-point scale, from 1 to 5, as described in <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_831">

2754Table 2</a>

2755. Calculation of metabolic tumor volume is an evolving approach that may enhance the prognostic utility of PET scans.[<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

27565</a>

2757] The COG and EuroNet definitions of PET response of lymph nodes or nodal masses are described in <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_832">

2758Table 3</a>

2759.</p>

2869</section>

2870</section>

2871<section id="_647">

2872<h3 id="_647_toc">

2873Establishing the Diagnosis of Hodgkin Lymphoma</h3>

2874<p id="_648" tabindex="-1">

2875After a careful physiological and radiographic evaluation of the patient, the least invasive procedure should be used to establish the diagnosis of lymphoma. However, this should not be interpreted to mean that a needle biopsy is the optimal methodology. Small fragments of lymphoma tissue are often inadequate for diagnosis, resulting in the need for second procedures that delay the diagnosis. </p>

2876<p id="_710" tabindex="-1">

2877If possible, the diagnosis should be established by biopsy of one or more peripheral lymph nodes. The likelihood of obtaining sufficient tissue should be carefully considered when selecting a biopsy procedure. Other issues to consider include the following:</p>

2878<div class="pdq-content-list">

2879<ul id="_1846">

2880<li>

2881Type of biopsy procedure.<div class="pdq-content-list">

2882<ul id="_1847" class="list-dash">

2883<li>

2884Aspiration cytology alone is not recommended because of the lack of stromal tissue, the small number of cells present in the specimen, and the difficulty of classifying Hodgkin lymphoma into one of the subtypes.</li>

2885<li>

2886An image-guided biopsy may be used to obtain diagnostic tissue from intra-thoracic or intra-abdominal lymph nodes. On the basis of the involved sites of disease, alternative procedures to consider may include thoracoscopy, mediastinoscopy, and laparoscopy. Thoracotomy or laparotomy is rarely needed to access diagnostic tissue.</li>

2887<li>

2888Because bone marrow involvement is relatively rare in pediatric Hodgkin lymphoma patients, bilateral bone marrow biopsy should be performed only in patients with advanced disease (stage III or stage IV) and/or B symptoms.[<a href="#cit/section_4.20">

288920</a>

2890]<p id="_1848" tabindex="-1">

2891A meta-analysis of nine clinical studies including both pediatric and adult patients showed that PET-CT achieved high sensitivity (96.9%) and high specificity (99.7%) in detecting bone marrow involvement in newly diagnosed patients with Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.17">

289217</a>

2893] In a consensus statement based on these studies, this group no longer recommends bone marrow biopsy in the initial evaluation of adults with Hodgkin lymphoma, with PET-CT being used instead to identify bone marrow involvement.[<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

28946</a>

2895] For more information, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/adult-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_12">

2896Stage Information for Adult HL</a>

2897 section in Hodgkin Lymphoma Treatment.</p>

2898</li>

2899</ul>

2900</div>

2901</li>

2902<li>

2903Procedure-related complications.<div class="pdq-content-list">

2904<ul id="_1849" class="list-dash">

2905<li>

2906Patients with large mediastinal masses are at risk of cardiac or respiratory arrest during general anesthesia or heavy sedation.[<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

290721</a>

2908] After careful planning with the anesthesiologist, peripheral lymph node biopsy or image-guided core-needle biopsy of mediastinal lymph nodes may be feasible using light sedation and local anesthesia before proceeding to more invasive procedures. </li>

2909<li>

2910If airway compromise precludes a diagnostic operative procedure, preoperative treatment with steroids or low-dose, localized radiation therapy should be considered, although the latter can be technically difficult if the patient cannot recline. Since preoperative treatment may affect the ability to obtain an accurate tissue diagnosis, a diagnostic biopsy should be obtained as soon as the risks associated with general anesthesia or heavy sedation are alleviated.</li>

2911</ul>

2912</div>

2913</li>

2914</ul>

2915</div>

2916</section>

2917<section id="_650">

2918<h3 id="_650_toc">

2919Lugano Staging Classification for Hodgkin Lymphoma</h3>

2920<p id="_651" tabindex="-1">

2921Stage is determined by anatomical evidence of disease using CT or MRI scanning in conjunction with functional imaging. The American Joint Committee on Cancer (AJCC) has adopted the Lugano classification to evaluate and stage lymphoma (see <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_557">

2922Table 4</a>

2923).[<a href="#cit/section_4.22">

292422</a>

2925] The Lugano classification system replaces the Ann Arbor classification system, which was adopted in 1971 at the Ann Arbor Conference,[<a href="#cit/section_4.23">

292623</a>

2927] with some modifications 18 years later from the Cotswolds meeting.[<a href="#cit/section_4.24">

292824</a>

2929] Staging is independent of the imaging modality used.</p>

3080<p id="_654" tabindex="-1">

3081Extralymphatic disease resulting from direct extension of an involved lymph node region is designated <em>

3082E</em>

3083. Extralymphatic disease can cause confusion in staging. For example, the designation <em>

3084E</em>

3085 is not appropriate for cases of widespread disease or diffuse extralymphatic disease (e.g., large pleural effusion that is cytologically positive for Hodgkin lymphoma), which should be considered stage IV. If pathological proof of noncontiguous involvement of one or more extralymphatic sites has been documented, the symbol for the site of involvement, followed by a plus sign (+), is listed. </p>

3086<p id="_1850" tabindex="-1">

3087Current practice is to assign a clinical stage on the basis of findings of the clinical evaluation; however, pathological confirmation of noncontiguous extralymphatic involvement is strongly suggested for assignment to stage IV.</p>

3088</section>

3089<section id="_30">

3090<h3 id="_30_toc">

3091Risk Stratification</h3>

3092<p id="_517" tabindex="-1">

3093After the diagnostic and staging evaluation data are acquired, patients are further classified into risk groups for treatment planning. The classification of patients into low-, intermediate-, or high-risk categories varies considerably among the pediatric research groups, and often even between different studies conducted by the same group, as summarized in <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_2057">

3094Table 5</a>

3095.[<a href="#cit/section_4.26">

309626</a>

3097]</p>
