3304<p id="_518" tabindex="-1">

3305Although all major research groups classify patients according to clinical criteria, such as stage and presence of B symptoms, extranodal involvement, or bulky disease, comparison of outcomes across trials is further complicated because of differences in how these individual criteria are defined.[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

33064</a>

3307]</p>

3308</section>

3309<section id="_519">

3310<h3 id="_519_toc">

3311Response Assessment</h3>

3312<p id="_520" tabindex="-1">

3313Further refinement of risk classification may be performed through assessment of response after initial cycles of chemotherapy or at the completion of chemotherapy.</p>

3314<section id="_521">

3315<h4 id="_521_toc">

3316Interim response assessment</h4>

3317<p id="_658" tabindex="-1">

3318The interim response to initial therapy, which may be assessed on the basis of volume reduction of disease, functional imaging status, or both, is an important prognostic variable in both early- and advanced-stage pediatric Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.27">

331927</a>

3320,<a href="#cit/section_4.28">

332128</a>

3322]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.14">

332314</a>

3324][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3325Level of evidence B4</a>

3326]</p>

3327<p id="_814" tabindex="-1">

3328Definitions for interim response are variable and protocol specific but can range from 2-dimensional reductions in size of greater than 50% to the achievement of a complete response, with 2-dimensional reductions in size of greater than 75% or 80% or a volume reduction of greater than 95% by anatomical imaging or resolution of 18F-FDG PET avidity.[<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

33297</a>

3330,<a href="#cit/section_4.29">

333129</a>

3332,<a href="#cit/section_4.30">

333330</a>

3334]</p>

3335<p id="_522" tabindex="-1">

3336The rapidity of response to early therapy has been used in risk stratification to titrate therapy in an effort to augment therapy in higher-risk patients or to reduce therapy in rapidly responding patients, which might, in turn, reduce the risk of late effects while maintaining efficacy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.27">

333727</a>

3338,<a href="#cit/section_4.28">

333928</a>

3340,<a href="#cit/section_4.30">

334130</a>

3342,<a href="#cit/section_4.31">

334331</a>

3344]</p>

3345<section id="_655">

3346<h5 id="_655_toc">

3347Trials using interim response to titrate therapy</h5>

3348<p id="_1851" tabindex="-1">

3349Several studies have evaluated the use of interim response to titrate additional therapy:</p>

3350<div class="pdq-content-list">

3351<ol id="_1852">

3352<li>

3353The Pediatric Oncology Group used a response-based therapy approach consisting of dose-dense ABVE-PC (doxorubicin, bleomycin, vincristine, etoposide, prednisone, and cyclophosphamide) for intermediate-stage and advanced-stage patients, in combination with 21 Gy involved-field radiation therapy (IFRT).[<a href="#cit/section_4.30">

335430</a>

3355]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3356<ul id="_1853">

3357<li>

3358 The dose-dense approach permitted reduction in chemotherapy exposure in 63% of patients who achieved a rapid early response on CT imaging after three ABVE-PC cycles.</li>

3359<li>

3360The 5-year event-free survival (EFS) rates were comparable for rapid early responders (86%; treated with three cycles of ABVE-PC) and slow early responders (83%; treated with five cycles of ABVE-PC). All patients received 21 Gy of regional radiation therapy.</li>

3361</ul>

3362</div>

3363</li>

3364<li>

3365The Children's Cancer Group (CCG) (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00004010">

3366CCG-59704</a>

3367) evaluated response-adapted therapy featuring four cycles of the dose-intensive BEACOPP (bleomycin, etoposide, doxorubicin, cyclophosphamide, vincristine, procarbazine, prednisone) regimen followed by a sex-tailored consolidation for pediatric patients with stages IIB, IIIB with bulky disease, and IV Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.31">

336831</a>

3369] <div class="pdq-content-list">

3370<ol id="_1854" class="lower-alpha">

3371<li>

3372 For rapid early responding girls, an additional four courses of COPP/ABV (cyclophosphamide, vincristine, procarbazine, prednisone/doxorubicin, bleomycin, vinblastine) without IFRT was given in an effort to reduce breast cancer risk.</li>

3373<li>

3374Rapid early responding boys received two cycles of ABVD followed by IFRT.</li>

3375<li>

3376Slow early responders received four additional courses of BEACOPP and IFRT.<div class="pdq-content-list">

3377<ul id="_1855">

3378<li>

3379Rapid early response (defined by resolution of B symptoms and &gt;70% reduction in tumor volume) was achieved by 74% of patients after four BEACOPP cycles, and the 5-year EFS rate among the cohort was 94% (median follow-up, 6.3 years).</li>

3380</ul>

3381</div>

3382</li>

3383</ol>

3384</div>

3385</li>

3386<li>

3387The COG <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00025259">

3388AHOD0031 (NCT00025259)</a>

3389, <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01026220">

3390AHOD0831 (NCT01026220)</a>

3391, and <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00302003">

3392AHOD0431 (NCT00302003)</a>

3393 trials also used interim response to titrate therapy. The AHOD0031 trial was designed to evaluate this paradigm of care by randomly assigning patients to receive either standard or response-based therapy. For more information, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_575">

3394North American cooperative and consortium trial results</a>

3395 section.</li>

3396</ol>

3397</div>

3398</section>

3399</section>

3400<section id="_659">

3401<h4 id="_659_toc">

3402End of chemotherapy response assessment</h4>

3403<p id="_660" tabindex="-1">

3404Restaging is carried out after all initial chemotherapy is completed. It may be used to determine the need for consolidative radiation therapy. Key concepts to consider include the following:</p>

3405<div class="pdq-content-list">

3406<ul id="_661">

3407<li>

3408<strong>

3409Defining complete response.</strong>

3410 The definition of complete response may vary by cooperative group or protocol.<div class="pdq-content-list">

3411<ul id="_662" class="list-dash">

3412<li>

3413The International Working Group (IWG) defined complete response for adults with Hodgkin lymphoma in terms of complete metabolic response as assessed by 18F-FDG PET, even when a persistent mass is present.[<a href="#cit/section_4.32">

341432</a>

3415] These criteria were endorsed in the Lugano Classification, with the recommendation for a 5-point scale to assess response.[<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

34166</a>

3417,<a href="#cit/section_4.33">

341833</a>

3419] COG protocols have adopted this approach for defining complete response. </li>

3420<li>

3421Previous studies have varied in the use of findings from the clinical examination, anatomical imaging, and functional imaging to assess response. Although complete response can be defined as absence of disease by clinical examination and/or imaging studies, complete response in Hodgkin lymphoma trials is often defined by a greater than 80% reduction of disease and a change from initial positivity to negativity on functional imaging.[<a href="#cit/section_4.34">

342234</a>

3423] This definition is necessary in Hodgkin lymphoma because fibrotic residual disease is common, particularly in the mediastinum. In some studies, such patients are designated as having an unconfirmed complete response. </li>

3424<li>

3425The EuroNet Hodgkin lymphoma trials use a similar early response assessment definition of PET positivity, which is a Deauville score of greater than 3 after two cycles of OEPA (vincristine [Oncovin], etoposide, prednisone, doxorubicin [Adriamycin]).[<a href="#cit/section_4.35">

342635</a>

3427]</li>

3428<li>

3429GPOH studies use stringent criteria for treatment group 1 (TG1) patients that include at least 95% reduction in tumor volume or less than 2 mL residual volume on CT. Patients achieving these metrics will have radiation therapy omitted. Treatment group 2 (TG2) and treatment group 3 (TG3) patients received radiation therapy despite their potential morphological <em>

3430complete response</em>

3431 (see <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_2057">

3432Table 5</a>

3433).[<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

34347</a>

3435]</li>

3436<li>

3437 The COG <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT02166463">

3438AHOD1331 (NCT02166463)</a>

3439 initial therapeutics clinical trial for patients with high-risk Hodgkin lymphoma uses 18F-FDG PET assessment, graded by a 5-point visual scale or Deauville criteria after two chemotherapy cycles, to define a rapid early-responding lesion for which radiation will be omitted. A mass of any size is permitted for a complete response designation if the PET is negative. The results of using the latter criteria are not yet available, so it may not be considered standard of care.</li>

3440</ul>

3441</div>

3442</li>

3443<li>

3444<strong>

3445Timing of PET scanning after completing therapy.</strong>

3446 Timing of PET scanning after completing therapy is an important issue. <div class="pdq-content-list">

3447<ul id="_703" class="list-dash">

3448<li>

3449For patients treated with chemotherapy alone, PET scanning is ideally performed a minimum of 3 weeks after the completion of therapy, while patients whose last treatment modality was radiation therapy should not undergo PET scanning until 8 to 12 weeks postradiation.[<a href="#cit/section_4.32">

345032</a>

3451]</li>

3452</ul>

3453</div>

3454</li>

3455<li>

3456<strong>

3457Screening frequency and overscreening.</strong>

3458 <div class="pdq-content-list">

3459<ul id="_1937" class="list-dash">

3460<li>

3461A COG study evaluated surveillance CT and detection of relapse in intermediate-stage and advanced-stage Hodgkin lymphoma. Most relapses occurred within the first year after therapy and were detected based on symptoms, laboratory, or physical findings. The method of detection of late relapse, whether by imaging or clinical change, did not affect overall survival. Routine use of CT at the intervals used in this study did not improve outcome.[<a href="#cit/section_4.36">

346236</a>

3463] Other investigations have supported the concept of reduced frequency of imaging.[<a href="#cit/section_4.37">

346437</a>

3465,<a href="#cit/section_4.38">

346638</a>

3467]</li>

3468<li>

3469Caution should be used in diagnosing relapsed or refractory disease based solely on anatomical and functional imaging because false-positive results are not uncommon.[<a href="#cit/section_4.39">

347039</a>

3471-<a href="#cit/section_4.41">

347241</a>

3473] Consequently, pathological confirmation of refractory or recurrent disease is recommended before modification of therapeutic plans.</li>

3474</ul>

3475</div>

3476</li>

3477</ul>

3478</div>

3479</section>

3480</section>

3595Schwartz CL, Constine LS, Villaluna D, et al.: A risk-adapted, response-based approach using ABVE-PC for children and adolescents with intermediate- and high-risk Hodgkin lymphoma: the results of P9425. Blood 114 (10): 2051-9, 2009.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=19584400&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=19584400&amp;dopt=Abstract">

3596[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

3597</li>

3598<li id="section_4.31">

3599Kelly KM, Sposto R, Hutchinson R, et al.: BEACOPP chemotherapy is a highly effective regimen in children and adolescents with high-risk Hodgkin lymphoma: a report from the Children's Oncology Group. Blood 117 (9): 2596-603, 2011.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=21079154&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=21079154&amp;dopt=Abstract">

3600[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

3601</li>

3602<li id="section_4.32">

3603Cheson BD, Pfistner B, Juweid ME, et al.: Revised response criteria for malignant lymphoma. J Clin Oncol 25 (5): 579-86, 2007.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=17242396&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=17242396&amp;dopt=Abstract">

3604[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

3605</li>

J Clin Oncol 32 (27): 3048-58, 2014.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=25113771&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=25113771&amp;dopt=Abstract">

3648

3649 <section id="_885">

3650

3651 <h2>

3652Special Considerations for the Treatment of Children With Cancer</h2>

3653

3654 <div id="_section_885" class="pdq-sections">

3655<p id="_886" tabindex="-1">

3656Cancer in children and adolescents is rare, although the overall incidence has been slowly increasing since 1975.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

36571</a>

3658] Children and adolescents with

3659cancer should be referred to medical centers that have a multidisciplinary team

3660of cancer specialists with experience treating the cancers that occur during

3661childhood and adolescence. This multidisciplinary team approach incorporates the skills

3662of the following health care professionals and others to ensure that children receive treatment, supportive care, and rehabilitation

3663that will achieve optimal survival and quality of life:</p>

3664<div class="pdq-content-list">

3665<ul id="_887">

3666<li>

3667Primary care physicians.</li>

3668<li>

3669Pediatric surgeons.</li>

3670<li>

3671Radiation oncologists. </li>

3672<li>

3673Pediatric medical oncologists and hematologists.</li>

3674<li>

3675Rehabilitation specialists.</li>

3676<li>

3677Pediatric nurse specialists.</li>

3678<li>

3679Social workers.</li>

3680<li>

3681Child life professionals.</li>

3682<li>

3683Psychologists.</li>

3684</ul>

3685</div>

3686<p id="_888" tabindex="-1">

3687For information about supportive care for children and adolescents with cancer, see the summaries on <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/information-summaries/supportive-care" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/information-summaries/supportive-care">

3688Supportive and Palliative Care</a>

3689.</p>

3690<p id="_889" tabindex="-1">

3691The American Academy of Pediatrics has outlined guidelines for pediatric cancer centers and their role in the treatment of

3692pediatric patients with cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_5.2">

36932</a>

3694] At these pediatric cancer centers, clinical trials are

3695available for most types of cancer that occur in children and

3696adolescents, and the opportunity to participate is offered to

3697most patients and their families. Clinical trials for children and adolescents with

3698cancer are generally designed to compare potentially better therapy with current standard

3699therapy. Most of the progress

3700made in identifying curative therapies for childhood cancers has been achieved

3701through clinical trials. Information about ongoing clinical trials is

3702available from the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials">

3703NCI website</a>

3704.</p>

3705<h6 do-not-show="toc">

3706References</h6>

3707<ol>

3708<li id="section_5.1">

3709Smith MA, Altekruse SF, Adamson PC, et al.: Declining childhood and adolescent cancer mortality. Cancer 120 (16): 2497-506, 2014.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=24853691&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=24853691&amp;dopt=Abstract">

3710[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

3711</li>

3712<li id="section_5.2">

3713American Academy of Pediatrics: Standards for pediatric cancer centers. Pediatrics 134 (2): 410-4, 2014. <a href="https://pediatrics.aappublications.org/content/134/2/410" title="https://pediatrics.aappublications.org/content/134/2/410">

3714Also available online</a>

3715. Last accessed May 19, 2023.</li>

3716</ol>

3823<section id="_560">

3824<h3 id="_560_toc">

3825History of Treatment for Hodgkin Lymphoma</h3>

3826<p id="_561" tabindex="-1">

3827Long-term survival has been achieved in children and adolescents with Hodgkin lymphoma using radiation, multiagent chemotherapy, and combined-modality therapy. In select cases of localized nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma, complete surgical resection may be curative and obviate the need for cytotoxic therapy.</p>

3828<p id="_711" tabindex="-1">

3829Treatment options for children and adolescents with Hodgkin lymphoma include the following:</p>

3830<div class="pdq-content-list">

3831<ol id="_562">

3832<li>

3833<strong>

3834Radiation therapy</strong>

3835 as a single modality. <div class="pdq-content-list">

3836<ul id="_664">

3837<li>

3838Recognition of the excess adverse effects of high-dose radiation therapy on musculoskeletal development in children motivated investigations of multiagent chemotherapy alone or with lower radiation doses (15&#8211;25.5 Gy) and reduced treatment volumes (involved sites). It also led clinicians to abandon the use of radiation as a single modality except in select situations.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

38391</a>

3840-<a href="#cit/section_6.3">

38413</a>

3842]</li>

3843<li>

3844 Radiation therapy alone may rarely be considered for adolescents and young adults with nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma.</li>

3845<li>

3846Recognition of the excess risk of cardiovascular disease and subsequent neoplasms in adult survivors who were treated for Hodgkin lymphoma during childhood led to the restriction of radiation therapy in contemporary trials and the reduction in volume and dose when used.[<a href="#cit/section_6.4">

38474</a>

3848,<a href="#cit/section_6.5">

38495</a>

3850]</li>

3851</ul>

3852</div>

3853 </li>

3854<li>

3855<strong>

3856Multiagent chemotherapy</strong>

3857.<div class="pdq-content-list">

3858<ul id="_564">

3859<li>

3860The establishment of the non&#8211;cross-resistant combinations of MOPP (mechlorethamine, vincristine [Oncovin], procarbazine, and prednisone) developed in the 1960s and ABVD (doxorubicin [Adriamycin], bleomycin, vinblastine, dacarbazine) developed in the 1970s made long-term survival possible for patients with advanced and unfavorable (e.g., bulky, symptomatic) Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.6">

38616</a>

3862,<a href="#cit/section_6.7">

38637</a>

3864]<p id="_1856" tabindex="-1">

3865 MOPP-related sequelae include a dose-related risk of infertility and subsequent myelodysplasia and leukemia.[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

38662</a>

3867,<a href="#cit/section_6.8">

38688</a>

3869] The use of MOPP-derivative regimens substituting less leukemogenic and gonadotoxic alkylating agents (e.g., cyclophosphamide) for mechlorethamine or restricting cumulative alkylating agent dose exposure reduces this risk.[<a href="#cit/section_6.9">

38709</a>

3871] However, COPP-based regimens (substituting cyclophosphamide for mechlorethamine) are not commonly used in contemporary treatment protocols because of the restricted availability of procarbazine in many parts of the world.</p>

3872<p id="_1857" tabindex="-1">

3873 ABVD-related sequelae include a dose-related risk of cardiopulmonary toxicity related to doxorubicin and bleomycin.[<a href="#cit/section_6.10">

387410</a>

3875-<a href="#cit/section_6.12">

387612</a>

3877] The cumulative dose of these agents is proactively restricted in pediatric patients to reduce this risk.</p>

3878</li>

3879<li>

3880In an effort to reduce chemotherapy-related toxicity, hybrid regimens alternating MOPP and ABVD or derivative therapy were developed that used lower total cumulative doses of alkylators, doxorubicin, and bleomycin.[<a href="#cit/section_6.13">

388113</a>

3882,<a href="#cit/section_6.14">

388314</a>

3884] </li>

3885<li>

3886Etoposide has been incorporated into treatment regimens as an effective alternative to alkylating agents, in an effort to reduce gonadal toxicity and enhance antineoplastic activity.[<a href="#cit/section_6.15">

388715</a>

3888]<p id="_1938" tabindex="-1">

3889 Etoposide-related sequelae include an increased risk of subsequent myelodysplasia and leukemia that appears to be rare when etoposide is used in restricted doses in pediatric Hodgkin lymphoma regimens.[<a href="#cit/section_6.16">

389016</a>

3891,<a href="#cit/section_6.17">

389217</a>

3893]</p>

3894</li>

3895<li>

3896More recent pediatric trials have used procarbazine-free standard backbone regimens, such as ABVE-PC (doxorubicin, bleomycin, vincristine, etoposide, prednisone, and cyclophosphamide) in North America [<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

389718</a>

3898,<a href="#cit/section_6.19">

389919</a>

3900] and OEPA-COPDAC (vincristine, etoposide, prednisone, doxorubicin; cyclophosphamide, vincristine,

3901prednisone,

3902dacarbazine) in Europe.[<a href="#cit/section_6.20">

390320</a>

3904] Both of these regimens represent dose-dense therapies that use six drugs to maximize intensity without exceeding thresholds of toxicity.</li>

3905</ul>

3906</div>

3907</li>

3908<li>

3909<strong>

3910Multiagent chemotherapy alone versus combined-modality therapy</strong>

3911.<div class="pdq-content-list">

3912<ul id="_565">

3913<li>

3914Treatment with non&#8211;cross-resistant chemotherapy alone offers advantages in developing countries lacking radiation facilities and trained personnel, as well as diagnostic imaging modalities needed for clinical staging. This treatment option also avoids the potential long-term growth inhibition, organ dysfunction, and solid tumor induction associated with radiation. </li>

3915<li>

3916Chemotherapy-alone treatment protocols usually prescribe higher cumulative doses of alkylating agent and anthracycline chemotherapy, which may produce acute- and late-treatment morbidity from myelosuppression, cardiac toxic effects, gonadal injury, and subsequent leukemia. However, more recent trials are designed to significantly reduce these risks, especially in those with chemotherapy-responsive disease.[<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

391718</a>

3918]</li>

3919<li>

3920In general, the use of combined chemotherapy and low-dose involved-site radiation therapy (LD-ISRT) broadens the spectrum of potential toxicities, while reducing the severity of individual drug-related or radiation-related toxicities. The results of prospective and controlled randomized trials indicate that combined-modality therapy, compared with chemotherapy alone, produces a superior event-free survival (EFS). However, because of effective second-line therapy, overall survival (OS) has not differed among the groups studied.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

392121</a>

3922,<a href="#cit/section_6.22">

392322</a>

3924]</li>

3925</ul>

3926</div>

3927</li>

3928</ol>

3929</div>

3930</section>

3931<section id="_566">

3932<h3 id="_566_toc">

3933Contemporary Treatment of Hodgkin Lymphoma</h3>

3934<p id="_567" tabindex="-1">

3935Contemporary treatment for pediatric patients with Hodgkin lymphoma uses a risk-adapted and response-based paradigm that assigns the length and intensity of therapy based on disease-related factors such as stage, number of involved nodal regions, tumor bulk, the presence of B symptoms, and early response to chemotherapy by functional and anatomical imaging. Age, sex, and histological subtype may also be considered in treatment planning.</p>

3936<p id="_1858" tabindex="-1">

3937Treatment options for childhood Hodgkin lymphoma include the following:</p>

3938<div class="pdq-content-list">

3939<ol id="_1859">

3940<li>

3941<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_203">

3942Radiation therapy</a>

3943.</li>

3944<li>

3945<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_665">

3946Chemotherapy</a>

3947.</li>

3948</ol>

3949</div>

3950<section id="_568">

3951<h4 id="_568_toc">

3952Risk designation</h4>

3953<p id="_1860" tabindex="-1">

3954Risk designation depends on favorable and unfavorable clinical features, as follows:</p>

3955<div class="pdq-content-list">

3956<ul id="_569">

3957<li>

3958<strong>

3959Favorable clinical features.</strong>

3960 These features include localized nodal involvement in the absence of B symptoms and bulky disease. Risk factors considered in other studies include the number of involved nodal regions, the presence of hilar adenopathy, the size of peripheral lymphadenopathy, and extranodal extension.[<a href="#cit/section_6.23">

396123</a>

3962]</li>

3963<li>

3964<strong>

3965Unfavorable clinical features. </strong>

3966These features include the presence of B symptoms, bulky mediastinal or peripheral lymphadenopathy, extranodal extension of disease, and advanced (stages IIIB&#8211;IV) disease.[<a href="#cit/section_6.23">

396723</a>

3968] In most clinical trials, bulky mediastinal lymphadenopathy is designated when the ratio of the maximum measurement of mediastinal lymphadenopathy to intrathoracic cavity on an upright chest radiograph equals or exceeds 33%. Notably, the definition of bulk is trial specific. For more information, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_638">

3969Definition of bulky disease</a>

3970 section.<p id="_1963" tabindex="-1">

3971Pleural effusions have been shown to be an adverse prognostic finding in patients treated for low-stage Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.24">

397224</a>

3973][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3974Level of evidence B4</a>

3975] The risk of relapse was 25% in patients with an effusion, as opposed to less than 15% in patients without an effusion. Patients with effusions were more often older (15 years vs. 14 years) and had nodular-sclerosing histology.</p>

3976<p id="_1861" tabindex="-1">

3977Localized disease (stages I, II, and IIIA) with unfavorable features may be treated similarly to advanced-stage disease in some treatment protocols or treated with therapy of intermediate intensity.[<a href="#cit/section_6.23">

397823</a>

3979]</p>

3980</li>

3981</ul>

3982</div>

3983<p id="_1862" tabindex="-1">

3984Inconsistency in risk categorization across studies often makes comparison of study outcomes challenging.</p>

3985</section>

3986<section id="_570">

3987<h4 id="_570_toc">

3988Risk-adapted treatment paradigms</h4>

3989<p id="_1863" tabindex="-1">

3990No single treatment approach is ideal for all pediatric and young adult patients because of differences in age-related developmental status and sex-related sensitivity to chemotherapy toxicity.</p>

3991<div class="pdq-content-list">

3992<ul id="_571">

3993<li>

3994The general treatment strategy for children and adolescents with Hodgkin lymphoma is chemotherapy, with or without radiation. <div class="pdq-content-list">

3995<ul id="_1864" class="list-dash">

3996<li>

3997The rapidity and degree of response may determine the number of cycles and intensity of chemotherapy as well as the radiation dose and volume. The primary exception to this strategy is in patients with nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma, when surgical resection has been advocated for stage I disease with a single resectable node in the United States [<a href="#cit/section_6.25">

399825</a>

3999] and for any resectable disease in Europe.[<a href="#cit/section_6.26">

400026</a>

4001]</li>

4002<li>

4003Sex-based regimens are considered because male patients are more vulnerable to gonadal toxicity from alkylating-agent chemotherapy, and female patients have a substantial risk of breast cancer after chest irradiation. In addition, males may experience a higher risk of cardiovascular disease after chest irradiation, which suggests that limiting radiation exposure is also desirable in males.[<a href="#cit/section_6.27">

400427</a>

4005]</li>

4006</ul>

4007</div>

4008</li>

4009</ul>

4010</div>

4011<p id="_1865" tabindex="-1">

4012Ongoing trials for patients with favorable disease are evaluating the effectiveness of treatment with fewer cycles of combination chemotherapy alone that limit doses of anthracyclines, alkylating agents, and radiation therapy. Contemporary trials for patients with intermediate/unfavorable disease are testing whether chemotherapy and radiation therapy can be limited in patients who achieve a rapid early response to dose-intensive chemotherapy regimens. Trials are also testing the efficacy of regimens integrating novel, potentially less-toxic agents such as brentuximab vedotin. </p>

4013</section>

4014<section id="_572">

4015<h4 id="_572_toc">

4016Histology-based therapy</h4>

4017<section id="_1814">

4018<h5 id="_1814_toc">

4019Nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma</h5>

4020<p id="_765" tabindex="-1">

4021The use of combination chemotherapy and/or radiation therapy can achieve excellent long-term progression-free survival (PFS) and OS in patients with nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.25">

402225</a>

4023,<a href="#cit/section_6.28">

402428</a>

4025,<a href="#cit/section_6.29">

402629</a>

4027] Late recurrences have been reported and are typically responsive to re-treatment. Because deaths observed among individuals with this histological subtype are frequently related to complications from cytotoxic therapy or transformation to non-Hodgkin lymphoma, risk-adapted treatment assignment is particularly important for limiting exposure to agents with established dose-related toxicities.[<a href="#cit/section_6.28">

402828</a>

4029,<a href="#cit/section_6.29">

403029</a>

4031]</p>

4032<p id="_573" tabindex="-1">

4033Histological subtype may direct therapy in patients with stage I, completely resected nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma, whose initial treatment may be surgery alone.[<a href="#cit/section_6.25">

403425</a>

4035]</p>

4036<p id="_712" tabindex="-1">

4037Evidence (surgery alone for localized nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma):</p>

4038<div class="pdq-content-list">

4039<ol id="_1866">

4040<li>

4041Although treatments for adult patients with nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma have traditionally involved high-dose radiation alone, chemotherapy plus LD-ISRT was originally used for treatment of this type of lymphoma in children. Standard of care in pediatric nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma at present is unclear but may include chemotherapy alone or, for limited disease, complete resection of isolated nodal disease without chemotherapy. Surgical resection of localized disease produces a prolonged disease-free survival in a substantial proportion of patients, obviating the need for immediate cytotoxic therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.25">

404225</a>

4043,<a href="#cit/section_6.26">

404426</a>

4045,<a href="#cit/section_6.30">

404630</a>

4047,<a href="#cit/section_6.31">

404831</a>

4049] Even if cytotoxic therapy is required, the possibility of avoiding chemotherapy and radiation in prepubertal children is advantageous.</li>

4050<li>

4051Results from a single-arm Children's Oncology Group (COG) trial support the strategy of observation after surgical resection of a single node and treatment with limited chemotherapy for children with favorable stage IA or IIA nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.25">

405225</a>

4053][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4054Level of evidence B1</a>

4055] To date, there is no evidence that this increases the risk of transformation to non-Hodgkin lymphoma.<div class="pdq-content-list">

4056<ul id="_1867">

4057<li>

4058 A total of 178 patients were treated with surgical resection alone for single-node disease (n = 52), chemotherapy alone after complete response (CR) to three cycles of AV-PC (doxorubicin, vincristine, prednisone, and cyclophosphamide) (n = 115), or chemotherapy with low-dose involved-field radiation therapy (LD-IFRT) (21 Gy) after incomplete response to AV-PC chemotherapy (n = 11). The 5-year EFS rate was 85.5%, and the OS rate was 100%.</li>

4059<li>

4060The 5-year EFS rate was 77% for patients observed after total resection and 88.8% for patients treated with AV-PC chemotherapy.</li>

4061</ul>

4062</div>

4063</li>

4064</ol>

4065</div>

4066<p id="_892" tabindex="-1">

4067Advanced-stage nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma is very rare. There is no consensus regarding the optimal treatment for this disease, although outcomes for patients are excellent when they are treated according to standard regimens for intermediate-risk or high-risk Hodgkin lymphoma.</p>

4068<p id="_1868" tabindex="-1">

4069Evidence (chemotherapy for nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma with unfavorable characteristics):</p>

4070<div class="pdq-content-list">

4071<ol id="_1869">

4072<li>

4073In a retrospective review of 41 patients with advanced-stage nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma, many different chemotherapy regimens were used; some included rituximab.[<a href="#cit/section_6.32">

407432</a>

4075][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4076Level of evidence C1</a>

4077]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4078<ul id="_1870">

4079<li>

4080 The OS rate was 98%, with the only death resulting from a subsequent neoplasm.</li>

4081</ul>

4082</div>

4083</li>

4084<li>

4085In a retrospective analysis, 97 intermediate-risk patients with nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma were treated on COG study <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00025259">

4086AHOD0031 (NCT00025259)</a>

4087.[<a href="#cit/section_6.33">

408833</a>

4089]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4090<ul id="_1871">

4091<li>

4092These patients demonstrated a higher CR rate than did patients with classical histology. The 5-year EFS rate was marginally superior in patients with nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma (91.2%) than in patients with classical Hodgkin lymphoma (83.2%).</li>

4093<li>

4094Most patients treated with four cycles of the ABVE-PC regimen achieved a rapid early response with a CR status and demonstrated excellent EFS and OS without IFRT. This finding suggests that the dose-dense, response-based protocol therapy designed for patients with classical Hodgkin lymphoma may have been more intensive than necessary for patients with nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma.</li>

4095</ul>

4096</div>

4097 </li>

4098</ol>

4099</div>

4100<p id="_2041" tabindex="-1">

4101Retrospective case series report on responses with rituximab alone [<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

410234</a>

4103] or in combination with cyclophosphamide, doxorubicin, vincristine, and prednisone (R-CHOP) [<a href="#cit/section_6.35">

410435</a>

4105] in adults with nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma. However, pediatric data have not been reported.</p>

4106<p id="_764" tabindex="-1">

4107A summary of treatment approaches for nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma can be found in <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_759">

4108Table 10</a>

4109. Both children and adults have a favorable outcome, particularly when the disease is localized (stage I), as it is for most patients.[<a href="#cit/section_6.25">

411025</a>

4111,<a href="#cit/section_6.26">

411226</a>

4113,<a href="#cit/section_6.30">

411430</a>

4115,<a href="#cit/section_6.36">

411636</a>

4117] In patients with nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma, transformation to aggressive large B-cell lymphoma rarely occurs. When transformation occurs, it substantially increases the risk of mortality.[<a href="#cit/section_6.37">

411837</a>

4119] In adults with nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma, a variant immunoarchitectural pattern has been associated with a higher risk of progression to aggressive lymphoma and more advanced disease.[<a href="#cit/section_6.38">

412038</a>

4121] Among long-term survivors of nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma, death is more likely to result from treatment-related toxicity (both acute and long-term) than from lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.39">

412239</a>

4123,<a href="#cit/section_6.40">

412440</a>

4125]</p>

4126</section>

4127<section id="_1816">

4128<h5 id="_1816_toc">

4129Mixed-cellularity Hodgkin lymphoma</h5>

4130<p id="_1817" tabindex="-1">

4131In addition to variable responses by histology for nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma, differences by mixed-cellularity histology have also been observed. COG investigators reported a 4-year EFS rate of 95.2% for children with stage I or stage II mixed-cellularity histology treated with minimal AV-PC therapy (and only rarely requiring radiation therapy), which was significantly better than the 75.8% EFS rate for patients who had nodular-sclerosing histology (<em>

4132P</em>

4133&#8201;=&#8201;.008).[<a href="#cit/section_6.41">

413441</a>

4135]</p>

4136</section>

4137</section>

4138</section>

4139<section id="_203">

4140<h3 id="_203_toc">

4141Radiation Therapy</h3>

4142<p id="_204" tabindex="-1">

4143As previously mentioned, most newly diagnosed children will be treated with risk-adapted chemotherapy alone or in combination with consolidative radiation therapy. Radiation therapy volumes can have variable and protocol-specific definitions, but they generally encompass lymph node sites initially involved at the time of diagnosis, without extensive inclusion of uninvolved regions, or positron emission tomography (PET)-avid sites at either interim or end-of-therapy assessment. Radiation therapy field reductions are made to account for tumor regression with chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.42">

414442</a>

4145]</p>

4146<section id="_205">

4147<h4 id="_205_toc">

4148Radiation volume</h4>

4149<p id="_529" tabindex="-1">

4150With advancements in systemic therapy, radiation therapy field definitions have become increasingly restricted. Radiation therapy is no longer needed to sterilize all disease. Advances in radiological imaging allow for a more precise radiation target definition. With effective chemotherapy and contemporary treatments using lower radiation doses (&lt;21 Gy) and reduced volumes (involved-site radiation therapy [ISRT]), contralateral uninvolved sites are not irradiated. </p>

4151<p id="_713" tabindex="-1">

4152General trends in radiation treatment volume are summarized as follows:</p>

4153<div class="pdq-content-list">

4154<ul id="_679">

4155<li>

4156Historical regional radiation therapy fields (e.g., mantle, subtotal, or total nodal) have been replaced by involved-nodal radiation therapy (INRT) or ISRT. In select situations, such as adolescents and young adults treated with radiation alone for nodular lymphocyte-predominant Hodgkin lymphoma, IFRT is used.</li>

4157<li>

4158INRT defines the treatment volume using the prechemotherapy PET&#8211;computed tomography (CT) scan that is obtained with the patient in a position that is similar to the position that will be used at the time of radiation therapy. This volume is later contoured onto the postchemotherapy-planning CT scan. The final treatment volume only includes the initially involved nodes with a margin, typically 2 cm.[<a href="#cit/section_6.43">

415943</a>

4160-<a href="#cit/section_6.45">

416145</a>

4162] The subsequent EuroNet-PHL-C2 trial employs INRT.</li>

4163<li>

4164ISRT, used in contemporary COG trials, is used when optimal prechemotherapy imaging (PET-CT in a position that is similar to what will be used at the time of radiation therapy) is not available to the radiation oncologist. Because the delineation of the area of involvement is less precise, a somewhat larger treatment volume is contoured than for INRT, typically at least 2 cm around the nodes where the lymphoma was located before chemotherapy was given. The exact volume will depend on the individual case scenario.[<a href="#cit/section_6.42">

416542</a>

4166] There are several situations in which this definition is further modified, such as when inappropriately large volumes of sensitive normal tissues might be exposed.[<a href="#cit/section_6.46">

416746</a>

4168]</li>

4169<li>

4170Modified involved-field radiation therapy (mIFRT) is the term used in the EuroNet-PHL-C1 trial to describe treatment volumes that contain the involved lymph node(s) as seen before chemotherapy plus radiation planning margins of 1 cm to 2 cm, depending on the area of involvement. These volumes are comparable to ISRT fields, although the development preceded the widespread availability of CT-based planning. </li>

4171</ul>

4172</div>

4173<p id="_720" tabindex="-1">

4174Breast-sparing radiation therapy plans using proton therapy are under evaluation to determine whether there is a statistically significant reduction in dose.[<a href="#cit/section_6.47">

417547</a>

4176] Ongoing studies seek to determine whether doses to other critical organs, such as the heart and lungs, can be reduced with proton therapy, without compromising survival outcomes.[<a href="#cit/section_6.48">

417748</a>

4178][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4179Level of evidence C1</a>

4180] Long-term results are pending.</p>

4181<section id="_680">

4182<h5 id="_680_toc">

4183ISRT or INRT treatment planning</h5>

4184<p id="_809" tabindex="-1">

4185Radiation therapy planning that uses CT scans obtained during the simulation procedure is a requirement for contemporary INRT or ISRT. Fusion of staging imaging (CT or PET-CT) with the planning CT dataset can facilitate delineation of the treatment volume. Radiation therapy planning scans that encompass the full extent of organs at risk (e.g., lungs) are important so that normal tissue exposures can be calculated accurately.</p>

4186<p id="_810" tabindex="-1">

4187Definitions that are important in planning radiation therapy include the following:</p>

4188<div class="pdq-content-list">

4189<ol id="_811">

4190<li>

4191Prechemotherapy or presurgery gross tumor volume (GTV): Imaging abnormalities of nodal or non-nodal tissues at initially involved sites.</li>

4192<li>

4193Postchemotherapy GTV: Imaging abnormalities at initially involved sites that remain abnormal after chemotherapy.</li>

4194<li>

4195Postchemotherapy clinical target volume (CTV): Abnormal tissues originally involved with lymphoma but taking into account the reduction in the axial (transverse) diameter that has occurred with chemotherapy. This delineation requires consideration of the expected routes of disease spread and the quality of pretreatment imaging.</li>

4196<li>

4197Internal target volume (ITV): Encompasses the CTV, with an added margin to account for variation in shape and motion within the patient (e.g., breathing).</li>

4198<li>

4199Planning target volume (PTV): Encompasses the ITV or CTV and accounts for variation in daily setup for radiation; generally, 0.5 cm to 1 cm.</li>

4200<li>

4201Boost radiation therapy: Some protocols, such as the EuroNet-PHL-C1 protocol, give additional radiation therapy (a boost) to sites with a poor response and/or bulky residual disease after initial chemotherapy. These volumes were determined after completion of all chemotherapy. This approach is sometimes used for patients with residual areas of PET avidity after chemotherapy.</li>

4202<li>

4203Organ at risk (OAR) determination and dose constraints: Because of the importance of long-term tissue injury after radiation, the dose to normal tissues is kept as low as reasonably achievable while adequately treating the PTV. Some specific organ radiation dose tolerances exist to guide these decisions, and these organs are considered <em>

4204organs at risk</em>

4205.</li>

4206</ol>

4207</div>

4208<p id="_812" tabindex="-1">

4209The treatment volume for unfavorable or advanced disease is somewhat variable and often protocol-specific. Large-volume radiation therapy may compromise organ function and limit the intensity of second-line therapy if relapse occurs. In patients with intermediate or advanced disease, who often have multifocal/extranodal disease, the current standard of therapy includes postchemotherapy ISRT that limits radiation exposure to large portions of the body.[<a href="#cit/section_6.42">

421042</a>

4211,<a href="#cit/section_6.46">

421246</a>

4213]</p>

4214</section>

4215</section>

4216<section id="_208">

4217<h4 id="_208_toc">

4218Radiation dose</h4>

4219<p id="_209" tabindex="-1">

4220The dose of radiation is also variously defined and often protocol-specific.</p>

4221<p id="_1872" tabindex="-1">

4222 General considerations regarding radiation dose include the following:</p>

4223<div class="pdq-content-list">

4224<ul id="_683">

4225<li>

4226Doses of 15 Gy to 25 Gy are typically used, with modifications based on patient age, the presence of bulky or residual (postchemotherapy) disease, and normal tissue concerns. Contemporary studies (Euronet-PHL-C1 and C2, AHOD1331, AHOD1721, and S1826) also allow for consideration of dose augmentation to 30 Gy to 36 Gy to residual PET-avid (Deauville score of 4 and, rarely, 5) sites after chemotherapy. This is because of the continued relapses in involved sites even after combined-modality therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

422718</a>

4228,<a href="#cit/section_6.49">

422949</a>

4230] </li>

4231<li>

4232Some protocols have prescribed a boost of 5 Gy to 10 Gy in regions with suboptimal response to chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

423350</a>

4234] This approach has not been formally evaluated to quantitate the risk-benefit relationship, and it clearly increases the risk of radiation-associated late effects on heart, lung, and breast tissues.</li>

4235</ul>

4236</div>

4237</section>

4238<section id="_210">

4239<h4 id="_210_toc">

4240Technical considerations</h4>

4241<p id="_684" tabindex="-1">

4242Technical considerations for the use of radiation therapy to treat Hodgkin lymphoma include the following:</p>

4243<div class="pdq-content-list">

4244<ul id="_685">

4245<li>

4246A linear accelerator with a beam energy of 6 mV is desirable because of its penetration, well-defined edge, and homogeneity throughout an irregular treatment field. </li>

4247<li>

4248<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=270731&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000270731&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet'); return(false);">

4249Three-dimensional conformal radiation therapy</a>

4250 (3-D CRT) or <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=335073&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000335073&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet'); return(false);">

4251intensity-modulated radiation therapy</a>

4252 (IMRT) are standard techniques in the treatment of lymphoma. Appropriate CT-based, image-guided treatment planning and delivery are standard, preferably with fusion of staging CT and PET imaging with radiation therapy planning CT datasets to delineate the target volumes.[<a href="#cit/section_6.42">

425342</a>

4254]</li>

4255<li>

4256Data are accumulating regarding the efficacy of IMRT and the decrease in median dose to normal surrounding tissues. Some uncertainty exists about the potential for increased late effects from IMRT, particularly subsequent neoplasms, because with IMRT, a larger area of the body receives a low dose compared with conventional techniques (although the mean dose to a volume may be decreased).</li>

4257<li>

4258Proton therapy is being investigated and may further decrease the mean dose to the surrounding normal tissue compared with IMRT or 3-D CRT, without increasing the volume of normal tissue receiving lower-dose radiation.[<a href="#cit/section_6.51">

425951</a>

4260]</li>

4261<li>

4262Individualized immobilization devices are preferable for young children to ensure accuracy and reproducibility.</li>

4263<li>

4264Attempts should be made to exclude or position breast tissue under the lung/axillary shielding. </li>

4265<li>

4266When the decision is made to include some or all of a critical organ (such as liver, kidney, or heart) in the radiation field, then normal tissue constraints are critical, depending on the chemotherapy used and patient age.</li>

4267<li>

4268Whole-lung irradiation (~10 Gy), with partial transmission blocks or intensity modulation, was historically a consideration in the setting of overt pulmonary nodules that had not achieved a CR.[<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

426918</a>

4270,<a href="#cit/section_6.19">

427119</a>

4272,<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

427350</a>

4274] However, it may be used in exceptional situations.</li>

4275</ul>

4276</div>

4277</section>

4278<section id="_212">

4279<h4 id="_212_toc">

4280Role of LD-ISRT in childhood and adolescent Hodgkin lymphoma</h4>

4281<p id="_537" tabindex="-1">

4282Because all children and adolescents with Hodgkin lymphoma receive chemotherapy, an important question is whether patients who achieve a rapid early response or a CR to chemotherapy require radiation therapy. Conversely, the judicious use of LD-ISRT may permit a reduction in the intensity or duration of chemotherapy below toxicity thresholds that would not be possible if single-modality chemotherapy were used, thus decreasing overall acute and late toxicities.</p>

4283<p id="_704" tabindex="-1">

4284The treatment approach for pediatric Hodgkin lymphoma should focus on maximizing disease control and minimizing risks of late toxicity associated with both radiation therapy and chemotherapy. Key points to consider regarding the role of radiation include the following:</p>

4285<div class="pdq-content-list">

4286<ul id="_686">

4287<li>

4288The use of LD-IFRT or ISRT in pediatric Hodgkin lymphoma may permit reduction in duration or intensity of chemotherapy and, thus, dose-related toxicity of anthracyclines, alkylating agents, and bleomycin. This may preserve cardiopulmonary and gonadal function and reduce the risk of subsequent leukemia.</li>

4289<li>

4290Radiation has been used as an adjunct to multiagent chemotherapy in clinical trials for low-, intermediate-, and high-risk pediatric Hodgkin lymphoma. The goal is to reduce risk of relapse in initially involved sites that do not show sufficient early or end-of-therapy responses to treatment, with the intent of preventing toxicity associated with second-line therapy.<p id="_1873" tabindex="-1">

4291Compared with chemotherapy alone, adjuvant radiation has, in most studies, produced a superior EFS for children with intermediate-risk and high-risk Hodgkin lymphoma who achieve a CR to multiagent chemotherapy. But it does not clearly improve OS because of the success of second-line therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.22">

429222</a>

4293]</p>

4294<p id="_1874" tabindex="-1">

4295 However, the intermediate-risk Hodgkin lymphoma study (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00025259">

4296AHOD0031 [NCT00025259]</a>

4297) did not show a benefit for IFRT in patients who achieved a rapid CR to chemotherapy (defined as &gt;60% reduction in 2-dimensional tumor burden after two cycles and metabolic remission and &gt;80% reduction after four cycles). The 4-year EFS rate was 87.9% for rapid responders who were randomly assigned to IFRT versus 84.3% (<em>

4298P</em>

4299 = .11) for rapid responders who were not assigned to IFRT. The OS rate was 98.8% in both groups.[<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

430018</a>

4301] In a subset analysis of patients with anemia and bulky limited-stage disease, the EFS rate was 89.3% for rapid early responders or complete remission patients who received IFRT, compared with 77.9% for patients who did not receive IFRT (<em>

4302P</em>

4303 = .019).[<a href="#cit/section_6.52">

430452</a>

4305][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4306Level of evidence B1</a>

4307]</p>

4308<p id="_1875" tabindex="-1">

4309Adjuvant radiation therapy may be associated with an increased risk of late effects or mortality.[<a href="#cit/section_6.53">

431053</a>

4311]</p>

4312</li>

4313<li>

4314Radiation consolidation may facilitate local disease control in individuals with refractory or recurrent disease, especially in those who have limited or bulky sites of disease progression/recurrence or persistent disease that does not completely respond to chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.54">

431554</a>

4316,<a href="#cit/section_6.55">

431755</a>

4318]</li>

4319<li>

4320The radiation dose to breast, heart, thyroid, and lung tissue received by patients in contemporary COG trials is 55% to 85% lower than the dose received by survivors analyzed in the Childhood Cancer Survivors Study (CCSS). This should be taken into consideration when estimating the risk of late toxicity associated with modern radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.56">

432156</a>

4322] However, a Stanford report identified a significant risk of breast cancer in children with Hodgkin lymphoma despite being treated with low-dose radiation therapy. The regimen used from 1970 to 1990 prescribed involved-field radiation therapy of 15 Gy to 25.5 Gy. At a median follow-up of 20.6 years, 18 of 110 children treated with radiation therapy in this dose range developed one or more subsequent malignant neoplasms, including 6 patients who developed breast carcinomas.[<a href="#cit/section_6.57">

432357</a>

4324]</li>

4325</ul>

4326</div>

4327<p id="_539" tabindex="-1">

4328Finally, an inherent assumption is made in a trial comparing chemotherapy alone versus chemotherapy and radiation that the effect of radiation on EFS will be uniform across all patient subgroups. However, it is not clear how histology, presence of bulky disease, presence of B symptoms, or other variables affect the efficacy of postchemotherapy radiation.</p>

4329</section>

4330</section>

4331<section id="_665">

4332<h3 id="_665_toc">

4333Chemotherapy</h3>

4334<p id="_667" tabindex="-1">

4335Many of the agents in original MOPP and ABVD regimens continue to be used in contemporary pediatric treatment regimens. Etoposide has been incorporated into some pediatric treatment regimens as an effective alternative to alkylating agents, in an effort to reduce gonadal toxicity and enhance antineoplastic activity. More recent pediatric trials have used procarbazine-free standard backbone regimens, such as ABVE-PC in North America [<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

433618</a>

4337,<a href="#cit/section_6.19">

433819</a>

4339] and OEPA-COPDAC in Europe.[<a href="#cit/section_6.20">

434020</a>

4341] Both of these regimens represent dose-dense therapies that use six drugs to maximize intensity without exceeding thresholds of toxicity.</p>

4342<p id="_666" tabindex="-1">

4343Combination chemotherapy regimens used in trials are summarized in <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_578">

4344Table 6</a>

4345.</p>

4346<table id="_578" class="table-default expandable-container">

4347<caption>

4375<tr>

4376<td colspan="5" valign="Middle">

4377<span class="sup">

4378a</span>

4379ABVE-PC modifications during the P9425 study included reducing bleomycin to 5 units/m<span class="sup">

43802</span>

4381 on day 0 and administering prednisone on days 0 to 7 (instead of days 0&#8211;9). In subsequent studies, doxorubicin dose was reduced to 25 mg/m<span class="sup">

43822</span>

4383 in all trials, and for high-risk Hodgkin lymphoma, use of cyclophosphamide was increased to 600 mg/m<span class="sup">

43842</span>

4385 on days 1 and 2.</td>
