8358<section id="_245">

8359<h3 id="_245_toc">

8360Male Gonadal Toxicity</h3>

8361<p id="_1924" tabindex="-1">

8362Important concepts related to male gonadal toxicity include the following:</p>

8363<div class="pdq-content-list">

8364<ul id="_593">

8365<li>

8366Gonadal irradiation and alkylating agent chemotherapy may produce testicular Leydig cell or germ cell dysfunction, with risk related to cumulative dose of both modalities. </li>

8367<li>

8368Hypoandrogenism associated with Leydig cell dysfunction may manifest as lack of sexual development; small, atrophic testicles; and sexual dysfunction. Hypoandrogenism also increases the risk of osteoporosis and metabolic disorders associated with chronic disease.[<a href="#cit/section_8.4">

83694</a>

8370,<a href="#cit/section_8.5">

83715</a>

8372]</li>

8373<li>

8374Testicular Leydig cells are relatively resistant to treatment toxicity compared with testicular germ cells. Survivors who are azoospermic after gonadal toxic therapy may maintain adequate testosterone production.[<a href="#cit/section_8.6">

83756</a>

8376-<a href="#cit/section_8.8">

83778</a>

8378]</li>

8379<li>

8380Infertility caused by azoospermia is the most common manifestation of gonadal toxicity. Some pubertal male patients will have impaired spermatogenesis before they begin therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.9">

83819</a>

8382,<a href="#cit/section_8.10">

838310</a>

8384]</li>

8385<li>

8386The prepubertal testicle is likely equally or slightly less sensitive to chemotherapy compared with the pubertal testicle. Pubertal status is not protective of chemotherapy-associated gonadal toxicity.[<a href="#cit/section_8.7">

83877</a>

8388,<a href="#cit/section_8.8">

83898</a>

8390]</li>

8391<li>

8392Chemotherapy regimens that do not include alkylating agents such as ABVD (doxorubicin [Adriamycin], bleomycin, vinblastine, dacarbazine), ABVE (doxorubicin [Adriamycin], bleomycin, vincristine, etoposide), OEPA (vincristine [Oncovin], etoposide, prednisone, doxorubicin [Adriamycin]), or VAMP (vincristine, doxorubicin [Adriamycin], methotrexate, prednisone) are not associated with male infertility.</li>

8393<li>

8394ABVE-PC (prednisone and cyclophosphamide) and OEPA-COPDAC (cyclophosphamide, vincristine,

8395prednisone,

8396dacarbazine) are titrated to limit alkylating agent dose to below the usual threshold associated with male sterility. Investigations evaluating germ cell function in relation to single alkylating agent exposure suggest that the incidence of permanent azoospermia will be low if the cyclophosphamide dose is less than 7.5 g/m<span class="sup">

83972</span>

8398.[<a href="#cit/section_8.8">

83998</a>

8400,<a href="#cit/section_8.11">

840111</a>

8402]</li>

8403<li>

8404Chemotherapy regimens that include more than one alkylating agent, usually procarbazine in conjunction with cyclophosphamide (i.e., COPP [cyclophosphamide, vincristine (Oncovin), prednisone, procarbazine]), chlorambucil, or nitrogen mustard (MOPP) confer a high risk of permanent azoospermia if treatment exceeds three cycles.[<a href="#cit/section_8.12">

840512</a>

8406,<a href="#cit/section_8.13">

840713</a>

8408]</li>

8409</ul>

8410</div>

8411<p id="_822" tabindex="-1">

8412For more information, see the <a href="/types/childhood-cancers/late-effects-hp-pdq#_380">

8413Testis</a>

8414 section in Late Effects of Treatment for Childhood Cancer.</p>

8415</section>

8416<section id="_249">

8417<h3 id="_249_toc">

8418Female Gonadal Toxicity</h3>

8419<p id="_1925" tabindex="-1">

8420Ovarian hormone production is linked to the maturation of primordial follicles. Depletion of follicles by alkylating agent chemotherapy can potentially affect both fertility and ovarian hormone production. Because of their greater complement of primordial follicles, the ovaries of young and adolescent girls are less sensitive to the effects of alkylating agents than are the ovaries of older women. In general, girls maintain ovarian function at higher cumulative alkylating agent doses, compared with the germ cell function maintained in boys.</p>

8421<p id="_1926" tabindex="-1">

8422Important concepts related to female gonadal toxicity include the following:</p>

8423<div class="pdq-content-list">

8424<ul id="_594">

8425<li>

8426Most females treated with contemporary risk-adapted therapy will have menarche (if prepubertal at treatment) or regain normal menses (if pubertal at treatment) unless pelvic radiation therapy is given without oophoropexy. Current regimens used in pediatric oncology are tailored to minimize the risk of ovarian failure. Data presented below related to pediatric treatment before 1987 [<a href="#cit/section_8.14">

842714</a>

8428,<a href="#cit/section_8.15">

842915</a>

8430] or adult trials in Europe (European Organisation for Research and Treatment of Cancer H1&#8211;H9 trials) [<a href="#cit/section_8.16">

843116</a>

8432] are not likely reflective of the expected reproductive outcomes in the current era.</li>

8433<li>

8434Ovarian transposition to a lateral or medial region from the planned radiation volume may preserve ovarian function in young and adolescent girls who require pelvic radiation therapy for lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_8.17">

843517</a>

8436] Ovarian transposition did not appear to modify risk of premature ovarian insufficiency in a cohort of 49 long-term survivors of Hodgkin lymphoma enrolled in the St. Jude Lifetime Cohort Study who were treated with gonadotoxic therapy and underwent ovarian transposition before pelvic radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.18">

843718</a>

8438]</li>

8439<li>

8440The risk of acute ovarian failure and premature menopause is substantial if treatment includes combined-modality therapy with alkylating agent chemotherapy and abdominal or pelvic radiation or dose-intensive alkylating agents for myeloablative conditioning before HSCT.[<a href="#cit/section_8.14">

844114</a>

8442,<a href="#cit/section_8.15">

844315</a>

8444] The risk of ovarian failure after treatment with contemporary regimens using lower cumulative doses of cyclophosphamide without procarbazine is anticipated to be lower.</li>

8445<li>

8446In the CCSS, investigators observed that Hodgkin lymphoma survivors were among the highest risk groups for acute ovarian failure and early menopause. In this cohort, the cumulative incidence of nonsurgical premature menopause among survivors treated with alkylating agents and abdominal or pelvic radiation approached 30%.[<a href="#cit/section_8.14">

844714</a>

8448,<a href="#cit/section_8.15">

844915</a>

8450] These patients were treated before 1986, usually with substantially higher doses of alkylating agents than are used in current regimens in the Children's Oncology Group, EuroNet, or other consortiums.</li>

8451<li>

8452A German study demonstrated that parenthood for female survivors of Hodgkin lymphoma was similar to that of the general population, although parenthood was lower for survivors who received pelvic radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.19">

845319</a>

8454]</li>

8455</ul>

8456</div>

8457<p id="_818" tabindex="-1">

8458For more information, see the <a href="/types/childhood-cancers/late-effects-hp-pdq#_387">

8459Ovary</a>

8460 section in Late Effects of Treatment for Childhood Cancer.</p>

8461</section>

8462<section id="_251">

8463<h3 id="_251_toc">

8464Thyroid Abnormalities</h3>

8465<p id="_1927" tabindex="-1">

8466Abnormalities of the thyroid gland, including hypothyroidism, hyperthyroidism, and thyroid neoplasms, occur at a higher rate among survivors of Hodgkin lymphoma than in the general population.</p>

8467<div class="pdq-content-list">

8468<ul id="_595">

8469<li>

8470<strong>

8471Hypothyroidism. </strong>

8472Risk factors for hypothyroidism include increasing dose of radiation, female sex, and older age at diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_8.20">

847320</a>

8474-<a href="#cit/section_8.22">

847522</a>

8476] CCSS investigators reported a 20-year actuarial risk of 30% of developing hypothyroidism in Hodgkin lymphoma survivors treated with 35 Gy to 44.99 Gy of radiation and 50% for subjects whose thyroid received 45 Gy or more of radiation.<p id="_1928" tabindex="-1">

8477Hypothyroidism develops most often in the first 5 years after treatment, but new cases have emerged more than 20 years after the cancer diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_8.21">

847821</a>

8479]</p>

8480</li>

8481<li>

8482<strong>

8483Hyperthyroidism. </strong>

8484Hyperthyroidism has been observed after treatment for Hodgkin lymphoma, with a clinical picture similar to that of Graves' disease.[<a href="#cit/section_8.23">

848523</a>

8486] Higher radiation dose has been associated with greater risk of hyperthyroidism.[<a href="#cit/section_8.21">

848721</a>

8488]</li>

8489<li>

8490<strong>

8491Subsequent neoplasms. </strong>

8492Thyroid neoplasms, both benign and malignant, have been reported with increased frequency after neck irradiation. The incidence of nodules varies substantially across studies (2%&#8211;65%) depending on the length of follow-up and detection methods used.[<a href="#cit/section_8.20">

849320</a>

8494-<a href="#cit/section_8.22">

849522</a>

8496] <p id="_803" tabindex="-1">

8497The relative risk (RR) of thyroid cancer is higher among Hodgkin lymphoma survivors (approximately 18-fold for the CCSS Hodgkin lymphoma cohort compared with the general population).[<a href="#cit/section_8.22">

849822</a>

8499] Risk factors for the development of thyroid nodules in Hodgkin lymphoma survivors reported by CCSS include time since diagnosis of more than 10 years (RR, 4.8; 95% confidence interval [CI], 3.0&#8211;7.8), female sex (RR, 4.0; 95% CI, 2.5&#8211;6.7), and radiation dose to thyroid higher than 25 Gy (RR, 2.9; 95% CI, 1.4&#8211;6.9).[<a href="#cit/section_8.22">

850022</a>

8501] The absolute risk of thyroid cancer is relatively low, with approximately 1% of the CCSS Hodgkin cohort developing thyroid cancer, with a median follow-up of approximately 15 years.[<a href="#cit/section_8.22">

850222</a>

8503] </p>

8504<p id="_813" tabindex="-1">

8505A single-institution Hodgkin lymphoma survivor cohort that included both adult and pediatric cases showed a cumulative incidence of thyroid cancer at 10 years from diagnosis of 0.26%, increasing to approximately 3% at 30 years from diagnosis. In this cohort, age younger than 20 years at Hodgkin lymphoma diagnosis and female sex were significantly associated with thyroid cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_8.24">

850624</a>

8507] </p>

8508</li>

8509</ul>

8510</div>

8511<p id="_819" tabindex="-1">

8512For more information, see the <a href="/types/childhood-cancers/late-effects-hp-pdq#_67">

8513Thyroid Gland</a>

8514 section in Late Effects of Treatment for Childhood Cancer summary.</p>

8515</section>

8516<section id="_300">

8517<h3 id="_300_toc">

8518Cardiac Toxicity</h3>

8519<p id="_301" tabindex="-1">

8520Hodgkin lymphoma survivors exposed to doxorubicin or thoracic radiation therapy are at risk of long-term cardiac toxicity. The effects of thoracic radiation therapy are difficult to separate from those of anthracyclines because few children undergo thoracic radiation therapy without the use of anthracyclines. The pathogenesis of injury differs, however, with radiation primarily affecting the fine vasculature of the heart, and anthracyclines directly damaging myocytes.[<a href="#cit/section_8.25">

852125</a>

8522-<a href="#cit/section_8.27">

852327</a>

8524]</p>

8525<p id="_1929" tabindex="-1">

8526 Survivors of childhood Hodgkin lymphoma older than 50 years will experience more than two times the number of chronic cardiovascular conditions and nearly five times the number of more severe (grades 3&#8211;5) cardiovascular conditions compared with community controls. Survivors will also, on average, have one severe, life-threatening, or fatal cardiovascular condition.[<a href="#cit/section_8.28">

852728</a>

8528]</p>

8529<p id="_1930" tabindex="-1">

8530 Cardiac mortality is higher for survivors of adolescent Hodgkin lymphoma than for survivors of young adult Hodgkin lymphoma. This finding was demonstrated in the Teenage and Young Adult Cancer Survivor Study cohort, with standardized mortality ratios (SMR) of 10.4 (95% CI, 8.1&#8211;13.3) for those diagnosed at age 15 and 19 years, compared with an SMR of 2.8 (95% CI, 2.3&#8211;3.4) for those diagnosed at age 35 to 39 years.[<a href="#cit/section_8.29">

853129</a>

8532]</p>

8533<section id="_596">

8534<h4 id="_596_toc">

8535Radiation-associated cardiovascular toxicity</h4>

8536<div class="pdq-content-list">

8537<ul id="_597">

8538<li>

8539Late effects of radiation to the heart may include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_8.30">

854030</a>

8541-<a href="#cit/section_8.32">

854232</a>

8543]<div class="pdq-content-list">

8544<ul id="_598" class="list-dash">

8545<li>

8546Delayed pericarditis.</li>

8547<li>

8548Pancarditis, including pericardial and myocardial fibrosis, with or without endocardial fibroelastosis.</li>

8549<li>

8550Cardiomyopathy.</li>

8551<li>

8552Coronary artery disease.[<a href="#cit/section_8.26">

855326</a>

8554,<a href="#cit/section_8.31">

855531</a>

8556]</li>

8557<li>

8558Functional valve injury.[<a href="#cit/section_8.26">

855926</a>

8560,<a href="#cit/section_8.33">

856133</a>

8562]</li>

8563<li>

8564Conduction defects.</li>

8565</ul>

8566</div>

8567 <p id="_599" tabindex="-1">

8568The risks to the heart are related to the amount of radiation delivered to different depths of the heart, volume and specific areas of the heart irradiated, total and fractional irradiation dose, age at exposure, and latency period.</p>

8569</li>

8570<li>

8571Modern radiation techniques allow a reduction in the volume of cardiac tissue incidentally exposed to higher radiation doses. It is anticipated that this will reduce the risk of adverse cardiac events.</li>

8572<li>

8573 Austrian-German investigators evaluated the development of cardiac disease (via patient self-report supplemented by physician report) in a cohort of 1,132 pediatric Hodgkin lymphoma survivors monitored for a median of 20 years. The 25-year cumulative incidence of heart disease increased with higher mediastinal radiation doses: 3% (unirradiated), 5% (20 Gy), 6% (25 Gy), 10% (30 Gy), and 21% (36 Gy). Valve defects were most common, followed by coronary artery disease, cardiomyopathy, rhythm disorders, and pericardial abnormalities.[<a href="#cit/section_8.33">

857433</a>

8575]</li>

8576<li>

8577In a study of adult survivors of Hodgkin lymphoma, vigorous exercise lowered the risk of cardiovascular events, independent of the treatment received.[<a href="#cit/section_8.34">

857834</a>

8579]</li>

8580<li>

8581Emerging data, not confined to patients with Hodgkin lymphoma but inclusive of other pediatric malignancies, suggest that a lower mean heart dose of 10 Gy to 15 Gy should be a goal in contemporary treatment protocols.[<a href="#cit/section_8.27">

858227</a>

8583]</li>

8584</ul>

8585</div>

8586</section>

8587<section id="_602">

8588<h4 id="_602_toc">

8589Anthracycline-related cardiac toxicity</h4>

8590<div class="pdq-content-list">

8591<ul id="_603">

8592<li>

8593Late complications related to anthracycline injury may include subclinical left ventricular dysfunction, cardiomyopathy, and congestive heart failure.[<a href="#cit/section_8.26">

859426</a>

8595]</li>

8596<li>

8597Increased risk of doxorubicin-related cardiomyopathy is associated with female sex, treatment with cumulative doses of 250 mg/m<span class="sup">

85982</span>

8599 or higher, younger age at time of exposure, and increased time from exposure.[<a href="#cit/section_8.35">

860035</a>

8601]</li>

8602<li>

8603Prevention or amelioration of anthracycline-induced cardiomyopathy is important because the continued usage of anthracyclines is required in cancer therapy in more than one-half of children with newly diagnosed cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_8.36">

860436</a>

8605,<a href="#cit/section_8.37">

860637</a>

8607] </li>

8608<li>

8609Dexrazoxane (a bisdioxopiperazine compound that readily enters cells and is subsequently hydrolyzed to form a chelating agent) has been shown to prevent heart damage in adults and children treated with anthracyclines.[<a href="#cit/section_8.38">

861038</a>

8611] Studies suggest that dexrazoxane is safe and does not interfere with chemotherapeutic efficacy. Dexrazoxane has been associated with increased hematologic toxicity and typhlitis in children with Hodgkin lymphoma receiving ABVE-PC chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.39">

861239</a>

8613]</li>

8614<li>

8615A number of trials have studied the risk of subsequent neoplasms following dexrazoxane administration, and none has found a significant association with subsequent neoplasms.[<a href="#cit/section_8.40">

861640</a>

8617,<a href="#cit/section_8.41">

861841</a>

8619] However, one study found a borderline statistical increase in subsequent neoplasms in patients randomly assigned to receive dexrazoxane. This increase was attributed to the administration of three topoisomerase inhibitors (doxorubicin, etoposide, and dexrazoxane) within 2 to 3 hours of each other.[<a href="#cit/section_8.42">

862042</a>

8621]</li>

8622<li>

8623Studies of cancer survivors treated with anthracyclines have not demonstrated the benefit of enalapril in preventing progressive cardiac toxicity.[<a href="#cit/section_8.43">

862443</a>

8625,<a href="#cit/section_8.44">

862644</a>

8627]</li>

8628</ul>

8629</div>

8630<p id="_820" tabindex="-1">

8631For more information, see the <a href="/types/childhood-cancers/late-effects-hp-pdq#_1360">

8632Late Effects of the Cardiovascular System</a>

8633 section in Late Effects of Treatment for Childhood Cancer.</p>

8634</section>

8635</section>

8636<section id="_253">

8637<h3 id="_253_toc">

8638Subsequent Neoplasms</h3>

8639<p id="_1931" tabindex="-1">

8640Series evaluating the incidence of subsequent neoplasms in survivors of childhood and adolescent Hodgkin lymphoma have been published.[<a href="#cit/section_8.45">

864145</a>

8642-<a href="#cit/section_8.52">

864352</a>

8644]; [<a href="#cit/section_8.53">

864553</a>

8646][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

8647Level of evidence C1</a>

8648] Many of the patients included in these series received high-dose radiation therapy and high-dose alkylating agent chemotherapy regimens, which are no longer used.</p>

8649<div class="pdq-content-list">

8650<ul id="_604">

8651<li>

8652Subsequent neoplasms comprise two distinct groups:[<a href="#cit/section_8.54">

865354</a>

8654,<a href="#cit/section_8.55">

865555</a>

8656]<div class="pdq-content-list">

8657<ul id="_1932" class="list-dash">

8658<li>

8659<strong>

8660Myelodysplasia and acute myeloid leukemia (AML)</strong>

8661 that are chemotherapy related.<p id="_1933" tabindex="-1">

8662 Subsequent hematological malignancy (most commonly AML and myelodysplasia) is related to the use of alkylating agents, anthracyclines, and etoposide and exhibit a brief latency period (&lt;10 years from the primary cancer).[<a href="#cit/section_8.56">

866356</a>

8664] This excess risk is largely related to cases of myelodysplasia and subsequent AML. </p>

8665<p id="_2000" tabindex="-1">

8666A single-study experience suggests that there could be an increase in malignancies when multiple topoisomerase inhibitors are administered in close proximity.[<a href="#cit/section_8.42">

866742</a>

8668]</p>

8669<p id="_2001" tabindex="-1">

8670 Clinical trials using dexrazoxane in childhood leukemia have not observed an excess risk of subsequent neoplasms.[<a href="#cit/section_8.42">

867142</a>

8672,<a href="#cit/section_8.57">

867357</a>

8674,<a href="#cit/section_8.58">

867558</a>

8676]</p>

8677<p id="_1934" tabindex="-1">

8678Chemotherapy-related myelodysplasia and AML are less prevalent after contemporary therapy because of the restriction of cumulative alkylating agent doses.[<a href="#cit/section_8.59">

867959</a>

8680,<a href="#cit/section_8.60">

868160</a>

8682]</p>

8683<p id="_2002" tabindex="-1">

8684Among 1,711 intermediate-risk Hodgkin lymphoma survivors treated with response-adapted therapy in the Children's Oncology Group (COG) <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00025259">

8685AHOD0031 (NCT00025259)</a>

8686 trial (median follow-up, 7.3 years), the 10-year cumulative incidence of subsequent malignancy was 1.3%, and the cumulative incidence of secondary myelodysplastic syndrome or AML was 0.2%. Of the three cases of secondary AML, the median time to onset was 2 years (range, 1.8&#8211;2.7 years).[<a href="#cit/section_8.61">

868761</a>

8688]</p>

8689</li>

8690<li>

8691<strong>

8692Solid neoplasms</strong>

8693 that are predominately radiation related.<p id="_1935" tabindex="-1">

8694 Solid neoplasms most often involve the skin, breast, thyroid, gastrointestinal tract, lung, and head and neck, with risk increasing with radiation dose.[<a href="#cit/section_8.50">

869550</a>

8696,<a href="#cit/section_8.52">

869752</a>

8698,<a href="#cit/section_8.62">

869962</a>

8700]; [<a href="#cit/section_8.53">

870153</a>

8702][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

8703Level of evidence C1</a>

8704] The risk of a solid subsequent neoplasm escalates with the passage of time after diagnosis of Hodgkin lymphoma, with a latency of 20 years or more. For more information about subsequent thyroid neoplasms, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_251">

8705Thyroid Abnormalities</a>

8706 section.</p>

8707<p id="_1936" tabindex="-1">

8708Breast cancer is the most common therapy-related, solid, subsequent neoplasm after Hodgkin lymphoma:</p>

8709<div class="pdq-content-list">

8710<ul id="_1946">

8711<li>

8712 The absolute excess risk of breast cancer ranges from 18.6 to 79 per 10,000 person-years, and the cumulative incidence ranges from 12% to 26%, with onset 25 to 30 years after radiation exposure.[<a href="#cit/section_8.49">

871349</a>

8714,<a href="#cit/section_8.63">

871563</a>

8716-<a href="#cit/section_8.65">

871765</a>

8718]</li>

8719<li>

8720High risk of breast cancer has been found to increase as early as 8 years after radiation exposure, is rare before age 25 years, and continues to increase with time from exposure. Importantly, breast cancer in female childhood cancer survivors typically develops at least 25 years earlier than in the general population and often years before the ages recommended for population-based screening.[<a href="#cit/section_8.49">

872149</a>

8722]</li>

8723<li>

8724The cumulative incidence of breast cancer by age 40 to 45 years ranges from 13% to 20%, compared with a 1% risk for women in the general population.[<a href="#cit/section_8.49">

872549</a>

8726,<a href="#cit/section_8.63">

872763</a>

8728,<a href="#cit/section_8.65">

872965</a>

8730,<a href="#cit/section_8.66">

873166</a>

8732] This risk is similar to that observed for women with a <em class="gene-name">

8733BRCA</em>

8734 gene mutation, for whom the cumulative incidence of breast cancer ranges from 10% to 19% by age 40 years.[<a href="#cit/section_8.67">

873567</a>

8736]</li>

8737</ul>

8738</div>

8739<p id="_2030" tabindex="-1">

8740Breast cancer risk after radiation therapy:</p>

8741<div class="pdq-content-list">

8742<ul id="_2031">

8743<li>

8744The risk of breast cancer in female survivors of Hodgkin lymphoma is directly related to the dose of radiation therapy received over a range from 4 Gy to 40 Gy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.68">

874568</a>

8746] Female patients treated with both radiation therapy and alkylating agent chemotherapy have a lower RR of developing breast cancer than women receiving radiation therapy alone.[<a href="#cit/section_8.50">

874750</a>

8748,<a href="#cit/section_8.69">

874969</a>

8750] </li>

8751<li>

8752CCSS investigators also demonstrated that breast cancer risk associated with breast irradiation was sharply reduced among women who received 5 Gy or more to the ovaries.[<a href="#cit/section_8.70">

875370</a>

8754] The protective effect of alkylating chemotherapy and ovarian radiation is believed to be mediated through induction of premature menopause, suggesting that hormone stimulation contributes to the development of radiation-induced breast cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_8.71">

875571</a>

8756]</li>

8757</ul>

8758</div>

8759<p id="_2032" tabindex="-1">

8760Breast cancer risk after chemotherapy (includes survivors of Hodgkin lymphoma and other childhood, adolescent, and young adult malignancies):</p>

8761<div class="pdq-content-list">

8762<ul id="_2033">

8763<li>

8764Several cohort studies have demonstrated a dose-related increased risk of breast cancer among female survivors of childhood, adolescent, and young adult cancer treated with anthracycline chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.72">

876572</a>

8766-<a href="#cit/section_8.75">

876775</a>

8768]</li>

8769<li>

8770St. Jude Lifetime Cohort investigators observed that treatment with anthracycline doses of 250 mg/m<span class="sup">

87712</span>

8772 or higher was associated with increased breast cancer risk in survivors who did not have pathogenic (or likely pathogenic) cancer-predisposing mutations and who did not receive chest radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.75">

877375</a>

8774]</li>

8775<li>

8776CCSS investigators reported an additive interaction between anthracyclines and chest radiation therapy, as the risk associated with this combination was higher than the sum of the individual risks.[<a href="#cit/section_8.73">

877773</a>

8778]</li>

8779<li>

8780Evidence for risk of breast cancer after treatment with higher doses of alkylating agents without chest radiation therapy has been conflicting, with one study reporting an increased risk and others not observing this effect.[<a href="#cit/section_8.72">

878172</a>

8782,<a href="#cit/section_8.73">

878373</a>

8784,<a href="#cit/section_8.76">

878576</a>

8786]</li>

8787<li>

8788The evidence is inconsistent about risks and dose thresholds for breast cancer after treatment with chemotherapy in women who did not receive chest radiation. Shared decision-making is recommended for planning breast cancer surveillance.[<a href="#cit/section_8.77">

878977</a>

8790]</li>

8791</ul>

8792</div>

8793</li>

8794</ul>

8795</div>

8796</li>

8797<li>

8798Hereditary syndromes, other than high-risk breast cancer syndromes, and pathogenic mutations may modify the effect of radiation exposure on breast cancer risk after childhood cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_8.78">

879978</a>

8800,<a href="#cit/section_8.79">

880179</a>

8802]</li>

8803<li>

8804A study of women survivors who received chest radiation for Hodgkin lymphoma showed that one of the most important factors in obtaining breast cancer screenings per guidelines was recommendation from their treating physician.[<a href="#cit/section_8.80">

880580</a>

8806] Standard guidelines for routine breast screening are available. The <a href="http://www.survivorshipguidelines.org/" title="http://www.survivorshipguidelines.org/">

8807COG guidelines</a>

8808 recommend annual screening with magnetic resonance imaging and mammograms for women beginning 8 years after treatment or at age 25 years, whichever is later.[<a href="#cit/section_8.80">

880980</a>

8810]</li>

8811</ul>

8812</div>

8813<p id="_821" tabindex="-1">

8814For more information, see the <a href="/types/childhood-cancers/late-effects-hp-pdq#_116">

8815Subsequent Neoplasms</a>

8816 section in Late Effects of Treatment for Childhood Cancer.</p>

8817</section>

9148 <section id="_154">

9149

9150 <h2>

9151Changes to This Summary (09/14/2023)</h2>

9152

9153 <div id="_section_154" class="pdq-sections">

9154<p id="_159" tabindex="-1">

9155The PDQ cancer information summaries are reviewed regularly and updated as

9156new information becomes available. This section describes the latest

9157changes made to this summary as of the date above.</p>

9158<p id="_2087" tabindex="-1">

9159<strong>

9160<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_15">

9161Diagnosis and Staging Information for Childhood Hodgkin Lymphoma</a>

9162</strong>

9163</p>

9164<p id="_2088" tabindex="-1">

9165Added Mhlanga et al. as <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_510">

9166reference 2</a>

9167.</p>

9168<p id="_2095" tabindex="-1">

9169The <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_650">

9170Lugano Staging Classification for Hodgkin Lymphoma</a>

9171 section was extensively revised.</p>

9172<p id="_2089" tabindex="-1">

9173<strong>

9174<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_32">

9175Treatment of Newly Diagnosed Children and Adolescents With Hodgkin Lymphoma</a>

9176</strong>

9177</p>

9178<p id="_2091" tabindex="-1">

9179Revised <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_578">

9180Table 6</a>

9181 to include the N-AVD chemotherapy regimen (nivolumab with doxorubicin, vinblastine, and dacarbazine) as an option for children and adolescents with Hodgkin lymphoma (cited Herrera et al. as reference 59).</p>

9182<p id="_2092" tabindex="-1">

9183Added <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_1878">

9184text</a>

9185 about the results of the S1826 phase III study that included both adolescents and adults who were randomly assigned to receive an AVD regimen with either brentuximab vedotin or nivolumab.</p>

9186<p id="_2093" tabindex="-1">

9187Revised <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_591">

9188Table 9</a>

9189 to add the N-AVD chemotherapy regimen and the results of the S1826 study.</p>

9190<p id="_disclaimerHP_3" tabindex="-1">

9191This summary is written and maintained by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/pediatric-treatment" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/pediatric-treatment">

9192PDQ Pediatric Treatment Editorial Board</a>

9193, which is

9194editorially independent of NCI. The summary reflects an independent review of

9195the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or NIH. More

9196information about summary policies and the role of the PDQ Editorial Boards in

9197maintaining the PDQ summaries can be found on the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_AboutThis_1">

9198About This PDQ Summary</a>

9199 and <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq">

9200PDQ&#174; Cancer Information for Health Professionals</a>

9201 pages.

9202</p>

9208

9209 <section id="_AboutThis_1">

9210

9211 <h2>

9212About This PDQ Summary</h2>

9213

9214 <div id="_section_AboutThis_1" class="pdq-sections">

9215<section id="_AboutThis_2">

9216<h3 id="_AboutThis_2_toc">

9217Purpose of This Summary</h3>

9218<p id="_AboutThis_3" tabindex="-1">

9219This PDQ cancer information summary for health professionals provides comprehensive, peer-reviewed, evidence-based information about the treatment of childhood Hodgkin lymphoma. It is intended as a resource to inform and assist clinicians in the care of their patients. It does not provide formal guidelines or recommendations for making health care decisions.</p>

9220</section>

9221<section id="_AboutThis_4">

9222<h3 id="_AboutThis_4_toc">

9223Reviewers and Updates</h3>

9224<p id="_AboutThis_5" tabindex="-1">

9225This summary is reviewed regularly and updated as necessary by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/pediatric-treatment" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/pediatric-treatment">

9226PDQ Pediatric Treatment Editorial Board</a>

9227, which is editorially independent of the National Cancer Institute (NCI). The summary reflects an independent review of the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or the National Institutes of Health (NIH).</p>

9228<p id="_AboutThis_22" tabindex="-1">

9229 Board members review recently published articles each month to determine whether an article should:</p>

9230<div class="pdq-content-list">

9231<ul id="_AboutThis_6">

9232<li>

9233be discussed at a meeting,</li>

9234<li>

9235be cited with text, or</li>

9236<li>

9237replace or update an existing article that is already cited.</li>

9238</ul>

9239</div>

9240<p id="_AboutThis_7" tabindex="-1">

9241Changes to the summaries are made through a consensus process in which Board members evaluate the strength of the evidence in the published articles and determine how the article should be included in the summary.</p>

9242<p id="" tabindex="-1">

9243The lead reviewers for Childhood Hodgkin Lymphoma Treatment are:</p>

9244<div class="pdq-content-list">

9245<ul id="">

9246<li>

9247Louis S. Constine, MD (James P. Wilmot Cancer Center at University of Rochester Medical Center)</li>

9248<li>

9249Alan Scott Gamis, MD, MPH (Children's Mercy Hospital)</li>

9250<li>

9251Thomas G. Gross, MD, PhD (National Cancer Institute)</li>

9252<li>

9253Melissa Maria Hudson, MD (St. Jude Children's Research Hospital)</li>

9254<li>

9255Kenneth L. McClain, MD, PhD (Texas Children's Cancer Center and Hematology Service at Texas Children's Hospital)</li>

9256<li>

9257Arthur Kim Ritchey, MD (Children's Hospital of Pittsburgh of UPMC)</li>

9258<li>

9259Lisa Giulino Roth, MD (Weil Cornell Medical College)</li>

9260<li>

9261Malcolm A. Smith, MD, PhD (National Cancer Institute)</li>

9262</ul>

9263</div>

9264<p id="_AboutThis_9" tabindex="-1">

9265Any comments or questions about the summary content should be submitted to Cancer.gov through the NCI website's <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us" title="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us">

9266Email Us</a>

9267. Do not contact the individual Board Members with questions or comments about the summaries. Board members will not respond to individual inquiries.</p>

9268</section>

9269<section id="_AboutThis_10">

9270<h3 id="_AboutThis_10_toc">

9271Levels of Evidence</h3>

9272<p id="_AboutThis_11" tabindex="-1">

9273Some of the reference citations in this summary are accompanied by a level-of-evidence designation. These designations are intended to help readers assess the strength of the evidence supporting the use of specific interventions or approaches. The PDQ Pediatric Treatment Editorial Board uses a <a href="/publications/pdq/levels-evidence/treatment">

9274formal evidence ranking system</a>

9275 in developing its level-of-evidence designations.</p>

9276</section>

9277<section id="_AboutThis_12">

9278<h3 id="_AboutThis_12_toc">

9279Permission to Use This Summary</h3>

9280<p id="_AboutThis_13" tabindex="-1">

9281PDQ is a registered trademark. Although the content of PDQ documents can be used freely as text, it cannot be identified as an NCI PDQ cancer information summary unless it is presented in its entirety and is regularly updated. However, an author would be permitted to write a sentence such as &#8220;NCI&#8217;s PDQ cancer information summary about breast cancer prevention states the risks succinctly: [include excerpt from the summary].&#8221;</p>

9282<p id="_AboutThis_14" tabindex="-1">

9283The preferred citation for this PDQ summary is:</p>

9284<p id="_AboutThis_15" tabindex="-1">

9285PDQ&#174; Pediatric Treatment Editorial Board. PDQ Childhood Hodgkin Lymphoma Treatment. Bethesda, MD: National Cancer Institute. Updated &lt;MM/DD/YYYY&gt;. Available at: <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq" title="https://www.cancer.gov/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq">

9286https://www.cancer.gov/types/lymphoma/hp/child-hodgkin-treatment-pdq</a>

9287. Accessed &lt;MM/DD/YYYY&gt;. [PMID: 26389170]</p>

9288<p id="_AboutThis_16" tabindex="-1">

9289Images in this summary are used with permission of the author(s), artist, and/or publisher for use within the PDQ summaries only. Permission to use images outside the context of PDQ information must be obtained from the owner(s) and cannot be granted by the National Cancer Institute. Information about using the illustrations in this summary, along with many other cancer-related images, is available in <a href="https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/" title="https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/">

9290Visuals Online</a>

9291, a collection of over 2,000 scientific images.

9292

9293</p>

9294</section>

9295<section id="_AboutThis_17">

9296<h3 id="_AboutThis_17_toc">

9297Disclaimer</h3>

9298<p id="_AboutThis_18" tabindex="-1">

9299Based on the strength of the available evidence, treatment options may be described as either &#8220;standard&#8221; or &#8220;under clinical evaluation.&#8221; These classifications should not be used as a basis for insurance reimbursement determinations. More information on insurance coverage is available on Cancer.gov on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care">

9300Managing Cancer Care</a>

9301 page.</p>

9302</section>
